Three Types of Signification in Words
Author divides signification into three types: mutabaqa, tadamun, iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of signification and explain why only mutabaqa and tadamun are used in definitions.

Core Idea
Signification is not monolithic: words convey meaning by correspondence, inclusion, or implication.

When a word signifies a meaning, it does so in one of three ways. The first two are essential for logical definition; the third is excluded because it leads to an infinite chain. Understanding this classification is the foundation of precise terminology in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
List mutabaqa, tadamun, iltizam. For each, note whether it signifies the whole meaning, a part, or a necessary concomitant.
2
Apply each type to an example
Take a word like 'house'. What does it signify by correspondence? By inclusion? By implication?
3
Analyse the definition rule
Why does the author exclude iltizam from definitions? What is the logical problem with allowing it?
4
Test with an Islamic term
Consider the word 'salat'. Does its definition rely only on mutabaqa and tadamun, or does it involve iltizam? (Example: salat by mutabaqa is the whole prayer; by tadamun includes the essential acts like ruku'; by iltizam might imply piety, but that is not used in the legal definition.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول من كتاب مقدمات القياس في دلالة الألفاظ وبيان وجوه دلالتها ونسبتها إلى المعاني وبيانه بسبعة تقسيمات : القسمة الأولى: أن نقول : الألفاظ تدلّ على المعاني من ثلاثة أوجه متباينة : الوجه الأول: الدلالة من حيث المطابقة كالاسم الموضوع بإزاء الشيء ، وذلك كدلالة لفظ الحائط على الحائط والآخر أن تكون بطريق التضمّن وذلك كدلالة لفظ البيت على الحائط ودلالة لفظ الإنسان على الحيوان وكذلك دلالة كلّ وصف أخصّ على الوصف الأعم الجوهري. الثالث . الدلالة بطريق الالتزام والاستتباع كدلالة لفظ السقف على الحائط ؛ فإنه مستتبع له استتباع الرفيق اللازم الخارج عن ذاته، ودلالة الإنسان على قابل صنعة الخياطة وتعلّمها، والمعتبر في التعريفات دلالة المطابقة والتضمن . فأما دلالة الالتزام فلا لأنها ما وضعها واضع اللغة بخلافهما، لأن المدلول فيها غير محدود ولا محصور، إذ لوازم الأشياء ولوازم لوازمها لا تنضبط ولا تنحصر فيؤدي إلى أن يكون اللفظ دليلاً على ما لا يتناهى من المعاني وهو محال .
The first art from the book Premises of Syllogism concerning the signification of words and clarifying the aspects of their signification and their relation to meanings, and its explanation in seven divisions: The first division: we say: words signify meanings in three distinct ways: the first way: signification by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), like a name established for a thing, such as the word 'wall' signifying the wall. The second: by way of inclusion (tadamun), such as the word 'house' signifying the wall, and the word 'human' signifying animal, and likewise the signification of every more specific description of the more general essential description. The third: signification by way of implication and entailment (iltizam), such as the word 'ceiling' signifying the wall; for it entails it as an accompanying necessary (lazim) external to its essence, and the signification of 'human' as capable of learning sewing. What is considered in definitions is the signification of correspondence and inclusion. As for implication, it is not, because the language setter did not establish it unlike the other two, since what is signified is not limited or bounded; for the concomitants of things and the concomitants of their concomitants cannot be confined or limited, leading to a word being evidence for an infinite number of meanings, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the ways in which a word signifies its meaning. The author introduces the first division of words, which concerns the relation between the word and its meaning. There are three types: correspondence (mutabaqa), where the word signifies the entire meaning for which it was coined, e.g., 'wall' for the whole wall; inclusion (tadamun), where the word signifies a part of its meaning, e.g., 'house' signifying the wall (since the wall is part of the house); and implication (iltizam), where the word signifies something that necessarily accompanies the meaning but is not part of it, e.g., 'ceiling' implying the wall (since a ceiling requires a wall). The author then states that only the first two are used in definitions because implication would lead to an infinite chain of necessary concomitants. For example, if a word signified all its implications, it would signify an infinite number of meanings, which is impossible. Therefore, in logical definitions, we rely on the whole meaning (mutabaqa) and its essential parts (tadamun). This is important in Islamic legal theory, where defining terms like 'salat' requires identifying its essential components rather than accidental concomitants.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة التضمن
دلالة الالتزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of signification is NOT used in definitions?
A) Dalala al-mutabaqa
B) Dalala al-tadamun
C) Dalala al-iltizam
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Words signify meanings in three ways, but only correspondence and inclusion are reliable for logical definitions, as implication leads to an endless chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the word 'wudu' (ablution). Identify what it signifies by mutabaqa, tadamun, and iltizam. Then explain why only the first two are relevant for its legal definition.

Dissecting the Logical Definition of the Noun
Author defines the noun with genus and five differentiae, explaining each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and five differentiae in the logical definition of the noun and explain what each excludes.

Core Idea
The five differentiae of the noun are exclusions, each separating it from a specific type of non-noun.

The logicians define the noun (ism) with a compound definition consisting of a genus and five differentiae. This definition is not arbitrary; each component serves to exclude something that might be mistaken for a noun. In this lesson, we will dissect the definition to see how each part works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term is 'ism' (noun). Note that this is the logical definition, not the grammatical one, though they overlap.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'sound' (sawt). This places the noun in the broad category of audible phenomena.
Is every sound a noun? No, that's why we need differentiae.
3
Analyze each differentia
There are five differentiae. For each, identify what it excludes: 'signifying' excludes non-signifying sounds like sneezes; 'by convention' excludes natural signs like dog barking; 'devoid of time' excludes verbs; 'its part does not indicate by itself' excludes sentences; 'indicates a determinate meaning' excludes negative expressions like 'la insan'.
Each differentia is a 'fasl' that separates the noun from other types of expressions.
4
Test the definition's completeness
Check whether the definition includes all nouns and excludes all non-nouns. For instance, do proper nouns like 'Zayd' satisfy all conditions? Yes, they are sounds, signify by convention, are timeless, their parts do not indicate alone (e.g., 'Zay' alone does not mean anything), and they have determinate meaning.
The author's five differentiae cover essential distinctions; no redundant or missing conditions.
5
Compare hadd vs rasm
This is a hadd (complete definition) because it gives genus and differentiae. A rasm (description) would only give properties without the genus, e.g., 'a word that signifies by convention without time' would be a rasm.
Understanding this distinction helps in evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاسم : صوت دال بتواطؤ (۲) مجرّد عن الزمان، والجزء من أجزائه لا يدلّ على انفراده ويدلّ على معنى محصل. ولما كان الحدّ مركباً من الجنس والفصول، وتذكر الفصول للاحترازات كان قولنا :صوت جنساً، وقولنا : دال : فصلاً يفصله عن العطاس والنحنحة والسعال وأمثالها وقولنا : بتواطؤ يفصله عن نباح الكلب؛ فإنه صوت دال على ورود وارد لكن لا بتواطؤ، وقولنا مجرّد عن الزمان احترازاً عن الفعل نحو قولنا : يقوم وقام وسيقوم ؛ فإن كلّ واحد صوت دال بتواطؤ . وقولنا الجزء من أجزائه لا يدلّ على انفراده احترازاً عن المركب التام كقولنا : زيد حيوان فإن هذا يُسمى خبراً وقولاً لا اسماً وقولنا : يدلّ على معنى محصل احترازاً عن الأسماء التي ليست محصلة كقولنا : لا إنسان، فإنه لا يُسمّى اسما أجزاء الحدّ فيه سوى هذا الاحتراز، فإن قولنا : لا إنسان قد مع وجود جميع يدلّ على الحجر والسماء والبقر، وبالجملة على كلّ شيء ليس بإنسان فليس له معنى محصل، إنما هو دليل على نفي الإنسان لا على إثبات شيء
The noun: a sound signifying by convention (2), devoid of time, and the part of its parts does not indicate by itself, and it indicates a determinate meaning. Since the definition is composed of genus and differentiae, and the differentiae are mentioned for exclusions, our saying 'sound' is the genus; our saying 'signifying' is a differentia separating it from sneezing, sniffling, coughing and the like; our saying 'by convention' separates it from the barking of a dog, for it is a sound signifying the arrival of an arrival but not by convention; our saying 'devoid of time' is an exclusion for the verb like our saying 'he stands', 'he stood', 'he will stand', for each is a sound signifying by convention; our saying 'the part does not indicate by itself' is an exclusion for the complete compound like our saying 'Zayd is an animal', for this is called a statement and a saying, not a noun; our saying 'it indicates a determinate meaning' is an exclusion for nouns that are not determinate like our saying 'no man', for it is not called a noun even with all the parts of the definition present except this exclusion, for our saying 'no man' indicates stone, sky, cow, and in general everything that is not a man, so it has no determinate meaning; rather it is an indication of the negation of man, not of the affirmation of something.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage provides the full logical definition (hadd) of the noun (ism). The author gives a compound definition with a genus and five differentiae. The genus is 'sound' (sawt). The first differentia 'signifying' (dal) excludes non-signifying sounds like sneezes. The second differentia 'by convention' (bi-tawatu') excludes natural sounds like dog barking that signify naturally. The third differentia 'devoid of time' (mujarrad 'an al-zaman) excludes verbs, which indicate time. The fourth differentia 'its part does not indicate by itself' (al-juz' min ajza'ihi la yadullu 'ala infiradihi) excludes complete compounds like 'Zayd is an animal', which are sentences. The fifth differentia 'indicates a determinate meaning' (yadullu 'ala ma'na muhassal) excludes words like 'la insan' (no man), which negate rather than affirm a determinate concept. This careful definition ensures that every necessary condition is included and all non-nouns are excluded.

Key Terms
اسم (ism): A sound signifying by convention, devoid of time, whose parts do not signify alone, indicating a determinate meaning. [Note: In later logic, the definition may drop 'sound' and emphasize 'word' (lafz) instead, but the core is stable.]
تواطؤ (tawāṭu’): Mutual agreement or convention; the word signifies by common consent of the speakers of the language. [Note: In philosophy of language, tawatu' sometimes contrasts with 'tabarru' (convention) but here it means conventional agreement.]
مُحَصَّل (muḥaṣṣal): Determinate, positive meaning; a word that affirms a concept rather than negating it. [Note: In later texts, muhassal may refer to 'acquired' meaning, but here it means 'determinate'.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'Dal' (signifying)
B) 'Bi-tawatu'' (by convention)
C) 'Mujarrad 'an al-zaman' (devoid of time)
D) 'Al-juz' min ajza'ihi la yadullu 'ala infiradihi' (its part does not indicate by itself)

Key Takeaway
The definition of the noun in Islamic logic is a model of how to use genus and differentiae to precisely delimit a concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the Arabic word 'shajarah' (tree). Verify that it satisfies all five conditions in the definition of the noun. Explain each condition briefly.
?

Distinguishing Noun, Verb, and Particle
Author defines the verb and particle, contrasting them with the noun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Arabic words as noun, verb, or particle based on the criteria of indication of time and predicative meaning.

Core Idea
The verb carries meaning in time and is always a predicate; the noun gives meaning without time; the particle depends on another word.

This lesson builds on the earlier definition of the noun and introduces the definitions of the verb and particle. The author sharpens your analytical eye by showing that time words like 'yesterday' are not verbs—they are nouns. Understanding these precise distinctions is essential for analyzing the structure of any Arabic sentence, especially in the Qur'an and classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts of speech
From the passage, list the three categories: noun (ism), verb (fi‘l), and particle (ḥarf). Note that the author uses 'kalima' as a synonym for verb in this context.
2
Extract the defining criteria for each
For the verb: it indicates a meaning and its occurrence in time, and it is always predicated of something. For the noun: it indicates a meaning without indicating time. For the particle: it indicates a meaning only when joined to another word.
3
Apply the criteria to test words
Take the examples given: 'ams' (yesterday) indicates time but is not a verb because it does not predicate a meaning; 'qama' (he stood) is a verb because it predicates standing in the past. Practice with other Arabic words.
Remember: time words like 'today' are nouns because they lack predication.
4
Understand the exception: non-predicative nouns
The author notes that some nouns can be determinate (like Zayd) or indeterminate (like 'la insan' when negated). This does not affect the core definition—nouns still lack time indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفعل : وهو الكلمة فإنه صوت دال بتواطؤ على الوجه الذي ذكرناه في الاسم، إنما يباينه في أنه يدلّ على معنى وقوعه في زمان كقولنا: قام ويقوم وليس يكفي في كونه فعلاً أن يدلّ على الزمان فحسب؛ فإن قولنا : أمس واليوم وغداً وعام أول ومضرب الناقة ومقدم الحاج يدلّ على الزمان، وليس بفعل، حيث أن الفعل يدلّ على معنى وزمان يقع فيه المعنى فيكون الفعل أبداً دليلاً على معنى محمول على غيره، فإذن الفرق بين الاسم والفعل تضمّن معنى الزمان فقط (۱) . وأما الحرف وهو الأداة فهو كلّ ما يدلّ على معنى لا يمكن أن يفهم بنفسه : ما لم يقدر اقتران غيره ،به مثل من وعلى وما أشبه ذلك. وقد أوجزت هذه الحدود فقيل في الاسم: إنه لفظ مفرد يدلّ على معنى من غير أن يدلّ على زمان ذلك المعنى من الأزمنة الثلاثة ثم منه ما هو محصل كزيد ومنه ما محصل كما إذا اقترن به حرف سلب فقيل : لا إنسان . وجود الكلمة : هي لفظة مفردة تدلّ على معنى وعلى الزمان الذي ذلك المعنى موجود فيه لموضوع ما غير معيّن، والحرف أو الأداة ما لا يدلّ على معنى إلَّا باقترانه بغيره .
As for the verb—it is the 'kalima'—it is a sound signifying by convention in the manner we mentioned for the noun, except that it differs in that it indicates a meaning and its occurrence in time, like our saying 'qama' (he stood) and 'yaqumu' (he stands). It is not sufficient for being a verb that it merely indicates time; for our saying 'ams' (yesterday), 'al-yawm' (today), 'ghadan' (tomorrow), ' 'am awwal' (last year), 'madrib al-naqa' (the place where the she-camel is struck), and 'muqaddam al-hajj' (the time of the coming of the pilgrim) indicate time but are not verbs, because the verb indicates a meaning and the time in which that meaning occurs; thus the verb is always an indicator of a meaning predicated on something else. Therefore, the difference between the noun and the verb is solely the inclusion of the meaning of time (1). As for the particle—which is the tool—it is everything that indicates a meaning that cannot be understood by itself unless the conjunction of something else to it is assumed, such as 'min', 'ala', and the like. These definitions have been summarized: it is said of the noun that it is a simple word indicating a meaning without indicating the time of that meaning from the three times; then some of it are determinate like 'Zayd', and some are not determinate as when a negation particle is attached to it, so it is said 'la insan'. The kalima: it is a simple word indicating a meaning and the time in which that meaning exists for an unspecified subject. The particle or tool: that which does not indicate a meaning except by conjunction with something else.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the verb (fi'l) and the particle (harf). The verb is a sound signifying by convention like the noun, but it specifically indicates a meaning together with its occurrence in time. The author emphasizes that merely indicating time is not enough—words like 'ams' (yesterday) indicate time but are not verbs because they do not predicate a meaning; they are nouns. The verb is always predicative: it carries a meaning that is attributed to something else (like 'he stood' implies someone who stood). The particle is defined as a word that cannot convey its meaning unless it is joined to another word, like prepositions 'min' (from) and 'ala' (on). The passage then summarizes: the noun is a simple word indicating a meaning without time; the verb is a simple word indicating a meaning and time for an unspecified subject; the particle indicates a meaning only when connected to another word.

Key Takeaway
A verb is a word that indicates a meaning occurring in time and is always predicated; a noun indicates meaning without time; a particle only conveys meaning in combination with another word.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Categories of Word-Meaning Relations
Author introduces four categories and defines the first: mushtarak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of word-meaning relations and distinguish mushtarak from the other three types.

Core Idea
Mushtarak: one word, multiple essences; Mutawati': one essence, graded intensity.

This passage introduces a foundational division in Islamic logic: how words relate to meanings. The author presents four categories: shared (mushtarak), univocal (mutawati'), synonymous (mutaradif), and different (mutazayila). We begin with the first, mushtarak, which is crucial for correctly interpreting texts, especially in Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
List the four categories
From the passage, identify the four Arabic terms: mushtarak, mutawati'a, mutaradifa, mutazayila.
These are the four 'stations' (manazil) mentioned.
2
Define mushtarak
The author defines mushtarak as 'the single word that is applied to different existents'. This is the core definition.
Aristotle referred to these as 'agreeing in name but differing in essence'.
3
Contrast with the other categories
While mushtarak has one word for multiple essences, mutawati' has one word for a single essence that admits gradation (e.g., 'white'), mutaradif has multiple words for one essence (synonyms), and mutazayila has different words for different essences.
The passage only defines mushtarak; the others are named but not described here.
4
Recognize the Aristotelian reference
The author quotes Aristotle's terminology: 'agreeing in name but differing in essence'. This is the standard definition of homonyms.
This shows the Islamic tradition's engagement with Greek logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة السادسة : في نسبة الألفاظ إلى المعاني : اعلم أن الألفاظ من المعاني على أربعة منازل : المشتركة والمتواطئة والمترادفة والمتزايلة . أما المشتركة (٢) : فهي اللفظ الواحد الذي يُطلق على موجودات مختلفة الألفاظ التي سمّاها أرسطو : المتفقة في الاسم المختلفة في الجوهر .
The sixth division: concerning the relation of words to meanings: Know that words are related to meanings in four stations: shared (al-mushtaraka), univocal (al-mutawāṭi’a), synonymous (al-mutarādifa), and different (al-mutazāyila). As for the shared (2): it is the single word that is applied to different existents—the words that Aristotle called: agreeing in name but differing in essence.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth division, which classifies words based on their relationship to meanings. Four categories are listed: mushtarak (shared/multivocal), mutawati' (univocal/nominal), mutaradif (synonymous), and mutazayila (different/diverse). The author begins defining the first, mushtarak: a single word that applies to multiple different existents. He references Aristotle's terminology: 'agreeing in name but differing in essence'. A classic example would be the word 'ayn, which can mean eye, spring, or essence. The other three categories are named but not yet defined in this passage.

Key Terms

مترادفة

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak words are homonyms: one word, multiple distinct essences, requiring careful context for correct interpretation.

The Four Relations of Generality and Specificity
Author presents the second division of existents into four relations: specific, general, equal, and mutual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four relations (more general, more specific, equal, mutual) holds between any two given terms in Islamic logical analysis.

Core Idea
Every pair of terms falls into one of four relations: general, specific, equal, or mutual.

When comparing two concepts or terms in Islamic logic, we need a precise way to describe how their extensions relate. This lesson introduces the four possible relations: one term may be more general, more specific, equal in extension, or partially overlapping with the other. Mastering this classification is essential for correct definition and analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being compared
Take any pair of meanings (ma'ani) or realities (haqa'iq) from an Islamic science. For example, 'zakat' and 'sadaqah'.
The terms must be well-defined in the discipline.
2
Check if one term includes all instances of the other
Ask: Does every instance of term A also fall under term B? If yes, A is more specific (akhass) and B is more general (a'amm). For instance, every 'zakat' is a 'sadaqah', but not vice versa: zakat is more specific.
This is the relation of absolute generality/specificity.
3
Check if the terms are co-extensive
If each term includes all instances of the other, they are equal (musawi). Example: 'animal' and 'sentient' in the author's classification (all animals are sentient and vice versa).
Equality is rare; it requires that the two definitions are extensionally identical.
4
Check for partial overlap
If neither term fully includes the other, they are mutual (a'amm min wajh wa akhass min wajh). Example: 'white' and 'animal': some animals are white (like swans), but not all; some white things are not animals (like gypsum).
This relation is common in fiqh when comparing 'illah rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الثانية للموجودات باعتبار نسبة بعضها إلى بعض بالعموم والخصوص : اعلم أن معنى من المعاني الموجودة، وحقيقة من الحقائق الثابتة إذا نسبتها إلى غيرها من تلك المعاني والحقائق، وجدتها بالإضافة إليه إما أعم وإما أخص وإما مساوياً، وإما أعم من وجه وأخص من وجه، فإنك إذا أضفت الإنسان إلى الحيوان وجدته أخص منه وإن أضفت الحيوان إلى الحسّاس وجدته مساوياً له لا أعم ولا أخص، وإن نسبت الأبيض إلى الحيوان وجدته أعم من وجه فإنه يشمل الجص والكافور وجملة من الجمادات، وأخص من وجه فإنه يقصر عن تناول الغراب والزنوج وجملة من الحيوانات فإذن جملة الحقائق تناسبها بهذا الاعتبار لا تعدو هذه الوجوه الأربعة، فقس على ما ذكرناه ما لم نذكره .
The second division of existents considering the relationship of some to others in terms of generality and specificity: Know that a meaning among existing meanings, and a reality among established realities, when you compare it to others of those meanings and realities, you find it in relation to it either more general, or more specific, or equal, or more general in one respect and more specific in another. For if you compare 'human' to 'animal', you find it more specific than it; and if you compare 'animal' to 'sentient', you find it equal to it, neither more general nor more specific; and if you compare 'white' to 'animal', you find it more general in one respect, for it includes gypsum, camphor, and a number of inanimate objects, and more specific in another respect, for it fails to include the raven, blacks, and a number of animals. So indeed, all realities in this respect do not exceed these four aspects. So analogize to what we have mentioned what we have not mentioned.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines the four possible relations between any two meanings or realities when compared in terms of generality and specificity. The author states that every meaning, when compared to another, falls into one of four categories: more general, more specific, equal, or more general in one way and more specific in another. He illustrates each with examples: 'human' is more specific than 'animal'; 'animal' is equal to 'sentient' (since all animals are sentient, and all sentient beings are animals, according to the author's classification); 'white' is more general than 'animal' in one respect (covering inanimate objects like gypsum and camphor) and more specific in another (excluding animals like ravens and black people that are not white). The author concludes that all realities, when compared, are reducible to these four relations, and that the reader should analogize other cases accordingly.

Distinguishing Genus, Differentia, and Species in Definitions
Author distinguishes genus, species, and differentia with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between genus, differentia, and species in a definition and identify whether a differentia is essential or accidental.

Core Idea
The differentia answers 'which kind?' not 'what is it?'—getting this wrong destroys precision in definitions.

This passage clarifies two common confusions: first, how knowledge relates to mental form, and second, how to distinguish between genus, differentia, and species. The author uses the example of wine to show that the differentia is not part of the answer to 'what is it?' but rather to 'which kind is it?'. This distinction is crucial for constructing accurate definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two questions (ma huwa vs. ayyu shay' huwa)
Notice that the author differentiates between asking 'what is it?' (genus) and 'which kind is it?' (differentia). This is the key to understanding the role of each.
2
Extract the genus and differentia from the wine example
The genus for wine is 'beverage' (sharab) and the differentia is 'intoxicant' (muskir). Together they form the definition in detail: 'intoxicating beverage'.
3
Classify the differentia as essential or accidental
The author distinguishes between essential differentia (like intoxicant for wine) that captures the essence, and accidental differentia (like 'red' or 'foaming') that are merely descriptive but not definitive.
4
Apply the concepts to another example
Try with 'human': genus 'animal' (hayawan), differentia 'rational' (natiq). Species is 'human' (insan) – a summary of genus and differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل إلَّا أن يكون كونه حيواناً حاضراً، ولا ماهية المثلث إلَّا أن يكون كونه شكلاً حاضراً فكذلك لا ينبغي أن تحصل صورة الشيء وحده في العقل، إلا أن يكون كونه موجوداً حاضراً في العقل، إن كان الوجود مقوماً للذات كالحيوانية للإنسان والشكلية للمثلث، وليس الأمر كذلك. وعلى الجملة وجود الشيء أما في الأعيان فيستدعي حضور جميع الذاتيات المقومة، وأما في الأذهان وهو مثال الوجود في الأعيان مطابق له وهو معنى العلم إذ لا معنى للعلم بالشيء إلا بثبوت صورة الشيء وحقيقته ومثاله في النفس، كما تثبت صورة الشيء في المرآة مثلاً إلَّا أن المرآة لا تثبت فيها إلّا أمثلة المحسوسات والنفس مرآة تثبت فيها أمثلة المعقولات، فيستدعي حضور جميع الذاتيات المقومة مرة أخرى .
فإن قال قائل : فقد عرفت الفرق بين الجنس وبين ما هو عام عموم الجنس وليس بجنس، فبماذا يعرف الفرق بين الفصل والنوع؟ قلنا : الفصل ذاتي لا يذكر في جواب ما هو بل يذكر في جواب أي شيء هو، فإنه يشار إلى الخمر مثلاً فيقال : ما هو ؟ فيذكر في الجواب : شراب ؛ فلا يحسن بعده أن يقال : ما هو؟ بل: أي شراب هو ؟ فيقال : مسكر ؛ فالمسكر فصل أي يفصله عن غيره وهو الذي يُسمّيه الفقهاء احترازاً، إلَّا أن الاحتراز قد يكون بالذاتي وقد يكون بغير الذاتي، وقد يخصص اسم الفصل عند الإطلاق بالذاتي . فلو قيل : أي شيء هو؟ وأجيب بأنه أحمر يقذف بالزبد ؛ فربما انفصل به عن غيره وحصل به الاحتراز ولكن يكون ذلك فصلاً غير ذاتي . وأما المُسكر ففصل ذاتي للشراب، وكذلك الناطق للحيوان . وعلى الجملة الجنس والفصل عبارة عن الحقيقة نفسها تفصيلاً كقولك : شراب مُسكر وحيوان ناطق ؛ والنوع عبارة عنها إجمالاً كقولك: إنسان وفرس وجمل سواء النوع الإضافي والحقيقي والفصل عبارة عن شيء ذي حقيقة كقولك : ناطق وحسّاس ومُسكر أي شيء ذو نطق وذو حس وذو إسكار، فكان الشيء الذي ورد عليه الوصف بـ ذو وما بعدها لم يذكر بالفصول القائلة ناطق وحسّاس ومُسكر. وسيأتي لهذا مزيد بيان في كتاب الحد الموصل إلى تصوّر حقائق الأشياء، إذ لا يتم الحدّ إلا بذكر الجنس والفصل.
Nor the essence of the triangle except that its being a shape is present. Likewise, the form of a thing alone should not be obtained in the mind, except that its being existent is present in the mind, if existence were constitutive of the essence, like animality for man and shape for triangle, but it is not the case. In summary, the existence of a thing – either in the external world requires the presence of all essential constituents, and in minds, which is an example of existence in the external world corresponding to it, and that is the meaning of knowledge, for there is no meaning to knowledge of a thing except the establishment of the form, reality, and example of the thing in the soul, just as the form of a thing is established in a mirror for example, except that only examples of sensible things are established in the mirror, while the soul is a mirror in which examples of intelligible things are established, so it requires the presence of all essential constituents once again.
If someone says: 'You have known the difference between genus and that which is general in the way genus is general but is not a genus, so by what is the difference between differentia and species known?' We say: The differentia is essential and is not mentioned in answer to 'what is it?' but is mentioned in answer to 'which thing is it?' For example, wine is pointed to and one says: 'What is it?' and the answer mentions 'a beverage'; then it is not proper to say after that 'What is it?' but rather 'Which beverage is it?' so one says 'an intoxicant.' Thus the intoxicant is the differentia, i.e., it separates it from others, and it is what the jurists call 'precaution', except that precaution may be by the essential or by the non-essential, and the name 'differentia' when used absolutely is restricted to the essential. So if it were said: 'Which thing is it?' and he answers that it is red, throwing foam, perhaps it separates it from others and precaution is obtained, but that would be a non-essential differentia. As for the intoxicant, it is an essential differentia for the beverage, and likewise the rational for the animal. In summary, genus and differentia are an expression of the reality itself in detail, like your saying 'an intoxicating beverage' and 'a rational animal'; species is an expression of it in summary, like your saying 'man', 'horse', 'camel', whether the relative or real species; and the differentia is an expression of a thing possessing a reality, like your saying 'rational', 'sensitive', 'intoxicant', i.e., a thing possessing rationality, sensation, and intoxication; thus the thing to which the description 'possessing' and what follows applies is not mentioned by the differentiae in the phrase 'rational', 'sensitive', 'intoxicant'. Further explanation of this will come in the book on the definition leading to the conceptualization of the realities of things, for the definition is only completed by mentioning genus and differentia.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage first establishes the nature of knowledge: to know a thing is to have its form present in the mind, including all its essential constituents (dhatiyyat muqawwima). The author compares the mind to a mirror that reflects intelligible forms, requiring that all essential parts be present, just as existence in the external world requires them. Then the author addresses a question: how do we distinguish between differentia (fasl) and species (naw')? The answer: the differentia is essential and is not the answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa) but to 'which one is it?' (ayyu shay' huwa). Using the example of wine, the author shows that 'intoxicant' is the differentia because after saying 'beverage' (the genus) one asks 'which beverage?' and answers 'intoxicant'. This differentia can be essential (like intoxicant for beverage, rational for animal) or non-essential (like red, foaming for wine). By default, when the term 'fasl' is used absolutely, it means the essential differentia. Finally, the author gives a summary: genus and differentia together express the reality in detail (e.g., 'intoxicating beverage'), while the species expresses it in summary (e.g., 'wine'). The differentia itself is something possessing a quality (e.g., 'rational' means 'possessing rationality'). This will be expanded in the book on definition.

The Principle of Complete Definition and Absolute Specificity
Author establishes the rule for complete definition and presents absolute specificity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an answer to 'what is it?' is a complete definition (hadd) covering all essential constituents, and distinguish it from an incomplete one.

Core Idea
A complete definition must include both genus and differentia to match the thing exactly.

When asked 'what is it?', the proper response is to give the essence of the thing. But what counts as a proper response? The author lays down a principle: the answer must include all essential constituents. He then illustrates with examples and introduces the first type of answer: absolute specificity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The question 'ma huwa' requests the mahiyya (essence) of a thing. The answer must convey that essence.
2
Recognize the requirement
The answer must mention all essential constituents (al-dhatiyyat al-muqawwima). Omitting any part makes the answer incomplete.
Essential constituents are what make the thing what it is.
3
Examine the wine example
If asked 'what is wine?', answering 'beverage' is insufficient because it omits the differentia 'intoxicant'. The complete answer includes both genus and differentia.
The differentia distinguishes wine from other beverages.
4
Understand absolute specificity
Absolute specificity (al-khususiyya al-mutlaqa) means the answer is coextensive with the thing – neither broader nor narrower. It is the full limit (hadd) containing all essential constituents.
Example: 'intoxicating beverage expressed from grapes' exactly matches the essence of wine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهية
القسمة السادسة في أصناف الحقائق المذكورة في جواب السائل عن الماهية : اعلم أن قول القائل في الشيء ما هو طلب لماهية الشيء، ومن عرف الماهية وذكرها فقد أجاب. والماهية إنما تتحقق بمجموع الذاتيات المقومة للشيء، فينبغي أن يذكر المجيب جميع الذاتيات المقومة للشيء حتى يكون مجيباً، وذلك بذكر حده ، فلو ترك بعض الذاتيات لم يتم جوابه. فإذا أشار إلى خمر وقال : ما هو ؟ فقولك : شراب ليس بجواب مطابق لأنك أخللت ببعض الذاتيات وأتيت بما الأعم، بل ينبغي أن تذكر المُسكر. وإذا أشار إلى إنسان وقال : ما هو؟ فتقول : إنه إنسان؛ فإن قال : ما هو الإنسان؟ فجوابك : إنه حيوان ناطق مائت؛ وهو تمام حده، والمقصود أنه يجب أن تذكر ما يعمّه وغيره وما يخصه، لأن الشيء هو باجتماع ذلك وبه تتحصل ذاته فإذا ثبت هذا الأصل فالمذكور في جواب ما ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام :
أحدها : ما هو بالخصوصية المطلقة وذلك بذكر الحدّ لتعريف ماهية الشيء المذكور كما إذا قيل لك : ما الخمر ؟ فتقول : شراب مُسكر معتصر من العنب؛ وهذا يختص بالخمر ويطابقه ويساويه فلا هو أعم منه ولا هو أخص منه، بل ينعكس كل واحد منهما على الآخر، وهو مع المساواة جامع لجميع الذاتيات المقومة من الجنس والفصول، وهكذا نسبة كلّ حدّ شيء إلى اسمه
الثاني : ما هو بالشركة المطلقة مثل ما إذا سُئلت عن جماعة فيها فرس وإنسان وثور: ما هي؟ فعند ذلك لا يحسن إلا أن تقول حيوان؛ فأما الأعم من ذلك وهو الجسم فليس تمام الماهية المشتركة بينها، بل هو جزء الماهية فإن الجسم جزء من ماهية الحيوان، إذ الحيوان هو جسم ذو نفس حسّاس مُتحرّك، هذا حده وإنما الإنسان والفرس ونحوه أخص دلالة مما يشمل الجملة، وقد جعل الجملة كشيء واحدٍ فأخص ماهية مشتركة لها حيوان .
الثالث : ما يصلح أن يذكر على الخصوصية والشركة جميعاً، فإنك إذا سئلت عن جماعة هم زيد وخالد: ما هم؟ كان الذي يصلح أن يجاب به على الشرط المذكور: إنهم أناس. وكذلك إذا سُئل عن زيد وحده: ما هو؟ لا أن يقال : من هو؟ كان الجواب الصحيح إنه إنسان لأن الذي يفضل في زيد على كونه إنساناً من كونه طويلاً أبيض ابن فلان أو كونه رجلاً أو امرأة أو صحيحاً أو سقيماً أو كاتباً أو عالماً أو جاهلاً، كلّ ذلك أعراض ولوازم لحقته لأمور اقترنت به في أول خلقته أو طرأت عليه بعد نُشوّه (۱) ، ولا يمتنع علينا أن نقدر أضدادها بل زوالها منه ويكون هو ذلك الإنسان بعينه وليس كذلك نسبة الحيوانية إلى الإنسانية، ولا نسبة الإنسانية إلى الحيوانية، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : قد اقترن به في
The essence (mahiyya). The sixth division concerning the kinds of realities mentioned in the answer of the questioner about the essence. Know that the saying of someone about a thing 'what is it?' is a request for the essence of the thing, and whoever knows the essence and mentions it has answered. The essence is only actualized by the totality of the essential constituents of the thing; thus the respondent should mention all the essential constituents of the thing in order to be answering, and that is by mentioning its limit (hadd). If he omits some of the essential constituents, his answer is not complete. If he points to wine and says 'What is it?' your saying 'a beverage' is not a correct answer because you have neglected some essential constituents and brought what is more general; rather you should mention the intoxicant. And if he points to a man and says 'What is it?' you say 'He is a man'; if he says 'What is man?' your answer is 'He is a rational, mortal animal'—that is the entirety of his limit. The intended point is that you must mention what includes it and others and what is specific to it, because the thing is only by the combination of that and by it its essence is actualized. Now that this principle is established, what is mentioned in answer to 'what' divides into three divisions:
One of them: that which is by absolute specificity, and that is by mentioning the limit to define the essence of the mentioned thing, just as when it is said to you 'What is wine?' you say 'An intoxicating beverage expressed from grapes'; this is specific to wine, corresponds to it, and is equal to it, so it is neither more general nor more specific than it; rather each of the two converts to the other, and it is, along with equality, comprehensive of all the essential constituents from genus and differentiae. Such is the relation of every limit of a thing to its name.
The second: that which is by absolute commonality, like when you are asked about a group containing a horse, a man, and an ox: 'What are they?' In that case it is not proper except that you say 'an animal'. As for what is more general than that, namely 'body', it is not the complete shared essence among them; rather it is a part of the essence, for body is part of the essence of animal, since animal is a body possessing a sensitive moving soul—that is its limit. And man, horse, and the like are more specific in signification than what includes the group, and the group is treated as one thing, so the most specific shared essence for them is 'animal'.
The third: that which is suitable to be mentioned both specifically and commonly. For you, if you are asked about a group who are Zayd and Khalid: 'What are they?' that which is suitable to be answered according to the mentioned condition is 'They are persons'. Likewise, if Zayd alone is asked about: 'What is he?'—not 'Who is he?'—the correct answer is 'He is a man', because what is additional in Zayd beyond his being a man, such as being tall, white, son of so-and-so, or being a male or female, healthy or sick, writer or scholar or ignorant—all of that is accidents and concomitants that attached to him due to matters accompanying him at the beginning of his creation or that occur after his development; and it is not impossible for us to conceive their opposites, rather their removal from him, and he remains that same man. This is not the case for the relation of animality to humanity, nor the relation of humanity to animality, for it cannot be said that it accompanied him in...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth division concerning the types of realities mentioned in response to the question about the essence (mahiyya). The author first establishes that the question 'what is it?' seeks the essence, and a proper answer must include all essential constituents (dhatiyyat muqawwima). Using examples: for wine, saying only 'beverage' is insufficient because it omits the differentia 'intoxicant'; for man, the proper answer is 'rational, mortal animal'. Then the author divides the answer into three categories.

First: the answer with absolute specificity (al-khususiyya al-mutlaqa), which is the full limit (hadd) coextensive with the thing. Example: 'wine' defined as 'intoxicating beverage expressed from grapes' – this matches the thing completely, neither broader nor narrower, and includes all essential parts.

Second: the answer with absolute commonality (al-shirka al-mutlaqa), when asked about a diverse group. Example: asked about a horse, man, and ox together, the proper answer is 'animal', not 'body', because 'animal' is the most specific shared essence. 'Body' is only a part of that essence.

Third: the answer that works both specifically and commonly. Example: asked about Zayd and Khalid together, one says 'persons' (an external attribute? Actually 'anas' means people/humans). For Zayd alone, asked 'what is he?' (not 'who'), the correct answer is 'man' (insan), because his individualizing attributes (tall, white, etc.) are accidents that could be removed while he remains the same human. In contrast, animality and humanity are essential and cannot be removed; they are not accidents. This shows that 'man' is the proper species answer for an individual human.

The author emphasizes that the answer must include both the broader (common) and the specific (distinctive) aspects, as the thing is constituted by their combination.

Answers of Absolute Commonality and That Which Suits Both
Author presents the second and third types of answers to 'ma huwa'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an answer of absolute commonality (e.g., 'animal' for a mixed group) and an answer that suits both specificity and commonality (e.g., 'man' for an individual or a group of humans).

Core Idea
The appropriate answer depends on the scope of the question: for a group of diverse things, give the most specific shared essence; for a single thing, the answer can double as both specific and common.

Not every question about 'what is it?' demands a fully specific definition. Sometimes the question is about a group with different species, and the correct answer is the most specific shared essence, not the most general category. The author also presents a third type that fits both an individual and a group, using the species 'man' as the example.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second scenario
When asked about a group containing different species (horse, man, ox) 'what are they?', the correct answer is not the most general category (body), but the most specific shared essence (animal).
The author treats the group as a single thing for the purpose of the question.
2
Recognize why 'animal' and not 'body'
'Body' is only a part of the essence of animal; animal includes 'sensitive, moving body'. So 'animal' is the most specific essence common to all members.
'Animal' is a species under the genus 'body'.
3
Examine the third scenario
For a group of humans (Zayd and Khalid) or an individual human (Zayd), the answer 'man' (insan) is appropriate. It works for both because it indicates the species.
Accidental attributes like tallness or whiteness are not part of the essence.
4
Accidents vs. essence
Attributes that can be removed while the thing remains the same (e.g., tall, white) are accidents, not essential. The essential definition does not include them.
Man remains man even if these attributes change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهية
القسمة السادسة في أصناف الحقائق المذكورة في جواب السائل عن الماهية : اعلم أن قول القائل في الشيء ما هو طلب لماهية الشيء، ومن عرف الماهية وذكرها فقد أجاب. والماهية إنما تتحقق بمجموع الذاتيات المقومة للشيء، فينبغي أن يذكر المجيب جميع الذاتيات المقومة للشيء حتى يكون مجيباً، وذلك بذكر حده ، فلو ترك بعض الذاتيات لم يتم جوابه. فإذا أشار إلى خمر وقال : ما هو ؟ فقولك : شراب ليس بجواب مطابق لأنك أخللت ببعض الذاتيات وأتيت بما الأعم، بل ينبغي أن تذكر المُسكر. وإذا أشار إلى إنسان وقال : ما هو؟ فتقول : إنه إنسان؛ فإن قال : ما هو الإنسان؟ فجوابك : إنه حيوان ناطق مائت؛ وهو تمام حده، والمقصود أنه يجب أن تذكر ما يعمّه وغيره وما يخصه، لأن الشيء هو باجتماع ذلك وبه تتحصل ذاته فإذا ثبت هذا الأصل فالمذكور في جواب ما ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام :
أحدها : ما هو بالخصوصية المطلقة وذلك بذكر الحدّ لتعريف ماهية الشيء المذكور كما إذا قيل لك : ما الخمر ؟ فتقول : شراب مُسكر معتصر من العنب؛ وهذا يختص بالخمر ويطابقه ويساويه فلا هو أعم منه ولا هو أخص منه، بل ينعكس كل واحد منهما على الآخر، وهو مع المساواة جامع لجميع الذاتيات المقومة من الجنس والفصول، وهكذا نسبة كلّ حدّ شيء إلى اسمه
الثاني : ما هو بالشركة المطلقة مثل ما إذا سُئلت عن جماعة فيها فرس وإنسان وثور: ما هي؟ فعند ذلك لا يحسن إلا أن تقول حيوان؛ فأما الأعم من ذلك وهو الجسم فليس تمام الماهية المشتركة بينها، بل هو جزء الماهية فإن الجسم جزء من ماهية الحيوان، إذ الحيوان هو جسم ذو نفس حسّاس مُتحرّك، هذا حده وإنما الإنسان والفرس ونحوه أخص دلالة مما يشمل الجملة، وقد جعل الجملة كشيء واحدٍ فأخص ماهية مشتركة لها حيوان .
الثالث : ما يصلح أن يذكر على الخصوصية والشركة جميعاً، فإنك إذا سئلت عن جماعة هم زيد وخالد: ما هم؟ كان الذي يصلح أن يجاب به على الشرط المذكور: إنهم أناس. وكذلك إذا سُئل عن زيد وحده: ما هو؟ لا أن يقال : من هو؟ كان الجواب الصحيح إنه إنسان لأن الذي يفضل في زيد على كونه إنساناً من كونه طويلاً أبيض ابن فلان أو كونه رجلاً أو امرأة أو صحيحاً أو سقيماً أو كاتباً أو عالماً أو جاهلاً، كلّ ذلك أعراض ولوازم لحقته لأمور اقترنت به في أول خلقته أو طرأت عليه بعد نُشوّه (۱) ، ولا يمتنع علينا أن نقدر أضدادها بل زوالها منه ويكون هو ذلك الإنسان بعينه وليس كذلك نسبة الحيوانية إلى الإنسانية، ولا نسبة الإنسانية إلى الحيوانية، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : قد اقترن به في
The essence (mahiyya). The sixth division concerning the kinds of realities mentioned in the answer of the questioner about the essence. Know that the saying of someone about a thing 'what is it?' is a request for the essence of the thing, and whoever knows the essence and mentions it has answered. The essence is only actualized by the totality of the essential constituents of the thing; thus the respondent should mention all the essential constituents of the thing in order to be answering, and that is by mentioning its limit (hadd). If he omits some of the essential constituents, his answer is not complete. If he points to wine and says 'What is it?' your saying 'a beverage' is not a correct answer because you have neglected some essential constituents and brought what is more general; rather you should mention the intoxicant. And if he points to a man and says 'What is it?' you say 'He is a man'; if he says 'What is man?' your answer is 'He is a rational, mortal animal'—that is the entirety of his limit. The intended point is that you must mention what includes it and others and what is specific to it, because the thing is only by the combination of that and by it its essence is actualized. Now that this principle is established, what is mentioned in answer to 'what' divides into three divisions:
One of them: that which is by absolute specificity, and that is by mentioning the limit to define the essence of the mentioned thing, just as when it is said to you 'What is wine?' you say 'An intoxicating beverage expressed from grapes'; this is specific to wine, corresponds to it, and is equal to it, so it is neither more general nor more specific than it; rather each of the two converts to the other, and it is, along with equality, comprehensive of all the essential constituents from genus and differentiae. Such is the relation of every limit of a thing to its name.
The second: that which is by absolute commonality, like when you are asked about a group containing a horse, a man, and an ox: 'What are they?' In that case it is not proper except that you say 'an animal'. As for what is more general than that, namely 'body', it is not the complete shared essence among them; rather it is a part of the essence, for body is part of the essence of animal, since animal is a body possessing a sensitive moving soul—that is its limit. And man, horse, and the like are more specific in signification than what includes the group, and the group is treated as one thing, so the most specific shared essence for them is 'animal'.
The third: that which is suitable to be mentioned both specifically and commonly. For you, if you are asked about a group who are Zayd and Khalid: 'What are they?' that which is suitable to be answered according to the mentioned condition is 'They are persons'. Likewise, if Zayd alone is asked about: 'What is he?'—not 'Who is he?'—the correct answer is 'He is a man', because what is additional in Zayd beyond his being a man, such as being tall, white, son of so-and-so, or being a male or female, healthy or sick, writer or scholar or ignorant—all of that is accidents and concomitants that attached to him due to matters accompanying him at the beginning of his creation or that occur after his development; and it is not impossible for us to conceive their opposites, rather their removal from him, and he remains that same man. This is not the case for the relation of animality to humanity, nor the relation of humanity to animality, for it cannot be said that it accompanied him in...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth division concerning the types of realities mentioned in response to the question about the essence (mahiyya). The author first establishes that the question 'what is it?' seeks the essence, and a proper answer must include all essential constituents (dhatiyyat muqawwima). Using examples: for wine, saying only 'beverage' is insufficient because it omits the differentia 'intoxicant'; for man, the proper answer is 'rational, mortal animal'. Then the author divides the answer into three categories.

First: the answer with absolute specificity (al-khususiyya al-mutlaqa), which is the full limit (hadd) coextensive with the thing. Example: 'wine' defined as 'intoxicating beverage expressed from grapes' – this matches the thing completely, neither broader nor narrower, and includes all essential parts.

Second: the answer with absolute commonality (al-shirka al-mutlaqa), when asked about a diverse group. Example: asked about a horse, man, and ox together, the proper answer is 'animal', not 'body', because 'animal' is the most specific shared essence. 'Body' is only a part of that essence.

Third: the answer that works both specifically and commonly. Example: asked about Zayd and Khalid together, one says 'persons' (an external attribute? Actually 'anas' means people/humans). For Zayd alone, asked 'what is he?' (not 'who'), the correct answer is 'man' (insan), because his individualizing attributes (tall, white, etc.) are accidents that could be removed while he remains the same human. In contrast, animality and humanity are essential and cannot be removed; they are not accidents. This shows that 'man' is the proper species answer for an individual human.

The author emphasizes that the answer must include both the broader (common) and the specific (distinctive) aspects, as the thing is constituted by their combination.

ما يصلح للخصوصية والشركة

Key Takeaway
The answer to 'what is it?' must be the most specific shared essence fitting the scope; for a single species, the species-term serves both individual and group contexts.

Why 'Sensing, Moving' Cannot Replace 'Animal' as Genus
Author defends using 'animal' rather than 'sensing, moving' as genus in definition of human.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a definition's genus must directly signify the essence, using the distinction between mutabaqa and iltizam to evaluate proposed alternatives.

Core Idea
A definition's genus must directly capture the essence, not just a concomitant property.

In defining a species, selecting the correct genus is crucial. An objector proposes using 'sensing, moving voluntarily' instead of 'animal' for 'human', claiming it is essential and co-extensive. The author refutes this by analyzing how words signify meaning, distinguishing between direct correspondence (mutabaqa) and implication (iltizam). This lesson will walk you through the logical grounds for rejecting such alternatives, using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's alternative
The objector suggests 'sensing, moving voluntarily' as a substitute for 'animal' in the genus of 'human', arguing that it is an essential attribute (dhati) co-extensive with animal.
This is a common error: assuming co-extensiveness suffices for definition.
2
Understand the refutation: direct vs. implied indication
The author argues that 'sensing' and 'moving' directly signify only the powers, not the underlying essence. The indication to the body (the substance) is by implication (iltizam), not by direct correspondence (mutabaqa).
The analogy with 'white' is key: 'white' directly means a thing with whiteness, but the body is only implied.
3
Apply the rule of definitional indication
Definition requires words that indicate the quiddity by mutabaqa or tadmin (inclusion). Terms that indicate only by iltizam are 'distant properties' (khawass ba'ida) and are not acceptable in answering 'what is it?'
The examples of 'laughing' for human and 'angles equal two right angles' for triangle illustrate the same point.
4
Evaluate the objector's position
The objector's proposal fails because it does not provide a direct answer about the essence. Even though 'sensing, moving' may be co-extensive, it does not capture the quiddity directly.
The author's verdict: the alternative is 'unsound' (ghayr sadid) under the required condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك أمه سبب جعله إنساناً لو لم يكن لكان فرساً أو حيواناً آخر، وهو الحيوان بعينه، بل إن لم يكن إنساناً لم يكن أصلاً حيواناً لا ذاك بعينه ولا غيره، فإذن الإنسان هو الذاتي الأخير وهو الذي يُسمّى نوعاً أخيراً. فإن قال قائل : لم لا يجوز في القسم الثاني أن يقال: حسّاس ومتحرك بالإرادة بدل الحيوان وهو ذاتي مساوٍ للحيوان؟ قلنا : ذلك غير سديد على الشرط المطلوب، لأن المفهوم من الحسّاس والمتحرّك على سبيل المطابقة هو مجرد أنه له قوة حس أو حركة، كما أن مفهوم الأبيض أنه شيء له بياض؛ فأما ما ذلك الشيء وما حقيقة ذاته فغير داخل في مفهوم هذه الألفاظ إلا على سبيل الالتزام حتى لا يعلم من يعلم من اللفظ بل من طريق عقلي يدلّ على أن هذا لا يتصوّر إلا لجسم ذي نفس . فإذا سُئل عن جسم : ما هو ؟ فقلت : أبيض؛ لم تكن مجيباً، وإن كنا نعلم من وجه آخر أن البياض لا يحل إلا جسماً، ولكن نقول دلالة الأبيض على الجسم بطريق الالتزام وقد قدمنا أن المعتبر في دلالة الألفاظ طريق المطابقة والتضمّن، ولذلك لا يجوز الجواب عن الماهية بالخواص البعيدة، وإن كانت تدلّ بطريق الالتزام فلا يحسن أن يقال في جواب من يسأل عن ماهية الإنسان أنه الضحاك، وفي جواب من يسأل عن ماهية المثلث أنه المساوية زواياه لقائمتين، وإن كان يدلّ بطريق الالتزام.
That is the cause that makes it human; if it were not, it would be a horse or another animal, and it is the very animal. Rather, if it is not human, it is not animal at all, not that one nor another. So the human is the ultimate essential, which is called the ultimate species. If someone says: Why is it not permissible in the second division to say 'sensing, moving voluntarily' instead of 'animal', while it is an essential co-extensive with animal? We say: That is not sound under the required condition, because the concept of 'sensing' and 'moving' in terms of correspondence is merely that it has a power of sense or movement, just as the concept of 'white' is that it is a thing with whiteness. As for what that thing is and its quiddity, it is not included in the concept of these words except by way of implication, so that one who knows the word does not know except through a rational way that indicates that this cannot be conceived except for a body with a soul. So if one asks about a body: 'What is it?' and you say 'white', you have not answered, even if we know from another aspect that whiteness only inheres in a body. But we say the indication of 'white' to the body is by way of implication, and we have previously stated that the consideration in the indication of words is the way of correspondence and inclusion. Therefore, it is not permissible to answer about quiddity with distant properties, even if they indicate by implication. So it is not good to say in answer to one who asks about the quiddity of human that it is 'the laughing one', nor in answer to one who asks about the quiddity of triangle that it is 'the one whose angles equal two right angles', even if it indicates by implication.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of a definitional point. The author has established that 'human' is the ultimate species, and its immediate genus is 'animal'. An objector proposes that one could instead use 'sensing, moving voluntarily' as the genus, claiming it is essential and co-extensive with 'animal'. The author refutes this by distinguishing between essential attributes (like 'animal') that directly signify the quiddity, and attributes (like 'sensing, moving') that signify only a property or power, not the essence itself. Using the analogy of 'white' for body, he explains that such terms indicate the substance only by implication (iltizam), not by direct correspondence (mutabaqa) or inclusion (tadmin). Since the rules of definition require the answer to capture the quiddity by direct concepts, distant properties (khawass ba'ida) like 'laughing' for human or 'having interior angles equal to two right angles' for triangle are unacceptable even if they are necessarily attached. The lesson emphasizes that a proper genus must be a self-subsisting essence, not an accident or attribute.

For a definition to be valid, the genus must directly signify the essence; terms that indicate only by implication are insufficient.

The Second Type of Qiyas: Appropriate Description (Munasib)
Author describes the second type: khiyar al-munasib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate a munasib description in analogical reasoning and distinguish it from purely devotional rulings.

Core Idea
Munasib descriptions are rationally suitable causes, giving probable knowledge even without textual indication.

In this passage, the author explains the second type of analogical reasoning, where the shared description is 'appropriate' (munasib) for the ruling. This means the description has a rational connection to the purpose of the law. The example of nabidh (date wine) being forbidden like khamr (grape wine) due to intoxication illustrates how jurists use munasib to extend rulings. The passage also addresses a key objection: what if the original ruling is purely devotional? The author argues that the majority of rulings are based on benefits, making munasib a valid, though probabilistic, tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core analogy
Find the ruling being extended (forbiddenness of wine to nabidh) and the common attribute (intoxication). Recognise that this is an analogical argument from asl (wine) to far' (nabidh) with the illa being intoxication.
2
Understand 'munasib'
Determine why intoxication is appropriate: because it removes the intellect, which is essential for religious obligation. This aligns with a higher purpose (maslaha). Munasib means the description is rationally suitable for the ruling, even if not textually indicated.
3
Follow the dialectical objection
The opponent suggests that the prohibition of wine might be purely devotional (ta'abbudi), not based on cause. The proponent concedes possibility but argues that the majority of shari'a rulings are based on benefits, so presumption (zann) leans toward the attribute being the cause.
4
Evaluate the strength of the analogy
The conclusion is probable (zanni), not certain. Munasib is a weaker type of qiyas than those with explicit textual indication. Understand that this type of qiyas is valid in usul al-fiqh for deriving rulings in new cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن يكون ما فيه الاجتماع مناسباً للحكم، كقولنا : النبيذ مسكر فيحرم كالخمر. فإذا قيل : لِمَ قلتم : المُسكر يحرم؟ قلنا : لأنه يزيل العقل، الذي هو الهادي إلى الحق وبه يتمّ التكليف ؛ فهذا مناسب للنظر في المصالح فيقال : لا يمتنع أن يكون الشرع قد راعى سكر ما يعتصر من العنب على الخصوص تعبداً، أو أثبت التحريم لا لعلة السكر، بل تعبداً في خمر العنب من غير التفات إلى السكر، فكم من الأحكام التي هي تعبدية غير معقولة، فيقول : نعم هذا غير ممتنع، ولكن الأكثر في عادة الشرع اتباع المصالح . فكون هذا من قبيل الأكثر أغلب على الظنّ من كونه من قبيل النادر .
The second: that what contains the commonality is appropriate for the ruling, like our saying: Nabidh is intoxicating, so it is forbidden like wine. If it is said: Why did you say that the intoxicant is forbidden? We say: Because it removes the intellect, which is the guide to truth and by which obligation is fulfilled. This is appropriate for consideration of benefits. So it is said: It is not impossible that the Law has considered the intoxication of what is pressed from grapes specifically as an act of worship, or established prohibition not due to the cause of intoxication, but as an act of worship in the case of grape wine without regard to intoxication. For many rulings are devotional (ta'abbudi) and not rationally understood. He [the one making the analogy] says: Yes, this is not impossible, but the majority in the custom of the Law is following benefits. So this being of the majority type is more dominant in opinion than it being of the rare type.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of analogical reasoning, where the shared description is 'appropriate' (munasib) for the ruling, meaning that it has a rational suitability. The example is the analogy of nabidh (date wine) to khamr (grape wine) due to intoxication. The author presents a dialogue: the proponent argues that nabidh is intoxicating, so it should be forbidden like wine. When asked why intoxication is forbidden, he says it removes the intellect, which is essential for religious obligation. This is appropriate because it aligns with the purpose of preserving intellect (maqsad). A potential objection is that the law might be purely devotional (ta'abbudi) in the case of grape wine, not based on the cause of intoxication. The proponent responds that while that is possible, the majority of Islamic legal rulings are based on underlying benefits, not arbitrary devotion. Therefore, the presumption (zann) is that intoxication is the effective cause, and this type of analogy is valid despite not being explicitly textually indicated. The author acknowledges that this yields probable (zanni) knowledge, not certainty.

مُنَاسِب
تَعَبُّدِيّ

Key Takeaway

The Third Type: Effective Description (Ta'thir)
Author describes the third type of effective description (ta'thir) with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct an argument using ta'thir (proven effect) as a method for establishing a cause ('illa) in Islamic legal analogy.

Core Idea
A description's effect in another case can justify analogy even without rational suitability.

This lesson introduces the third type of shared description in identifying the 'illa for legal analogy. Unlike types relying on textual indication or rational appropriateness, ta'thir relies on demonstrating that the description has a proven effect (ta'thir) in another case, even if it is not intrinsically suitable. The author uses a Hanafi example about guardianship for an orphan girl to illustrate how minority (sighar) serves as the effective description, not orphanhood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared description and ruling
In the example, the ruling is guardianship (wilayah) over property. The shared description proposed by the Hanafi is minority (sighar). Note that the other description (orphanhood) is also present but not used as the cause.
The description must be shared between the original case (non-orphan minor) and the new case (orphan minor).
2
Locate the other case where the effect is proven
The Hanafi points to the case of a son (or non-orphan minor) where minority has a proven effect by consensus (ittifaq) in establishing guardianship. This consensus establishes the effect of the description.
Effect means that the description is recognized as the cause for the ruling in another instance.
3
Understand why the difference does not break the analogy
The objector may point out that the orphan girl is an orphan while the other case is not. The Hanafi counters that the difference (orphanhood) does not overcome the shared description because orphanhood has no proven effect in any agreed-upon context.
Ta'thir requires that the shared description has a proven effect; differences that do not affect the ruling are ignored.
4
Recognize the role of consensus in ta'thir
The author notes that the effect is known by agreement (ittifaq) of scholars, not necessarily from a textual indication. This distinguishes ta'thir from other types where the cause is textually indicated.
Consensus provides certainty that the description truly has an effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن يبين للوصف الجامع تأثيراً في موضع من غير مناسبة، كما يقول الحنفي في اليتيمة إنها صغيرة، ويولي عليها كغير اليتيمة، فيقال : فلِمَ عللت الولاية بالصغر؟ فيقول : لأن الصغر قد ظهر أثره بالاتفاق في غير اليتيمة وفي الابن. وقدر أن الوصف غير مناسب حتى يستمر المثال فلا ينبغي أن يقال : هذه يتيمة وتيك ليست بيتيمة ؛ فيقال : الافتراق في هذا لا يقاوم الاشتراك في وصف الصغر، وقد ظهر تأثيره في موضع، واليتم لم يظهر تأثيره بالاتفاق في موضع، نعم لو ثبت أن اليتيم لا يُولّى عليه في المال لتقاوم الكلام. ولو قيل : ظهر أثر اليتم أيضاً في دفع الولاية في موضع ، كما ظهر أثر الصغر في موضع، فعند ذلك يحتاج إلى الترجيح. وإن شئت مثلت هذا القسم بقياس العنب على الرُّطب و اجتماعهما في توقع النقصان. ويقدر أن ذلك لم يعرف بإضافة لفظية من الشارع، بل عرف باتفاق من الفريقين حتى لا يلتحق بمثال الإضافة.
The third: that the shared description is shown to have an effect in some context without being appropriate. For example, the Hanafi says about the orphan girl that she is a minor, so he appoints a guardian over her as with a non-orphan minor. It is said: Why did you make the cause of guardianship to be minority? He says: Because minority has shown its effect by agreement in the case of a non-orphan and in the case of a son. Even if the description is not appropriate (munasib), then the example continues. So it should not be said: This is an orphan and that is not an orphan. It is said: The separation in this does not overcome the sharing in the description of minority, and its effect has appeared in one context, whereas orphanhood has not shown its effect by agreement in any context. Yes, if it were established that an orphan is not placed under guardianship in property, then the argument would be balanced. And if it is said: The effect of orphanhood also appeared in removing guardianship in some context, as the effect of minority appeared in some context, then at that point it requires weighting. If you wish, you may illustrate this type by the analogy of grapes to fresh dates and their commonality in the expectation of decrease. And it is estimated that this is not known through a verbal addition from the Lawgiver, but known by agreement of the two parties so that it does not fall under the example of addition.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage describes the third type, where the shared description has a demonstrated effect (ta'thir) in another case, even if it is not rationally appropriate (munasib). The author gives the example of a Hanafi jurist who says that an orphan girl is a minor and therefore should have a guardian appointed over her property, just like any other minor. The cause (illa) is minority, not orphanhood. Although minority is not necessarily appropriate for guardianship (it could be argued that orphanhood is the relevant factor), the Hanafi argues that minority has proven its effect by consensus (ittifaq) in other cases, such as the case of a son or a non-orphan minor. The objection that the girl is an orphan while the other case is not is countered by noting that the difference (orphanhood) does not overcome the common description (minority) because minority has a proven effect, while orphanhood has no proven effect in any agreed-upon context. If orphanhood were proven to have an effect (e.g., removing guardianship), then a weighing (tarjih) would be needed. The author also mentions that this type can be illustrated by the analogy of grapes to fresh dates based on the expectation of decrease, even if that is not textually indicated but known by agreement of scholars. The key point is that the effect of the description has been established in another place, not that it is rationally suitable.

B) Because minority has a proven effect by consensus in other cases, while orphanhood does not.
C) Because the Lawgiver explicitly stated that minority is the cause.

Key Takeaway
Ta'thir is a valid method for establishing the 'illa in qiyas: a description with a proven effect in another case suffices, even if it is not rationally appropriate.

Fourth Type of Preponderance: Shared Description Dominant
Author explains the fourth type of preponderance in qiyas based on the custom of the Law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the fourth type of preponderance in qiyas, where the shared description is predominant and the differing attribute is known to be irrelevant due to the consistent practice of the Law.

Core Idea
When the Law consistently disregards an attribute, that attribute cannot obstruct a valid analogy.

This lesson introduces the fourth type of preponderance (tarjih) in analogical reasoning (qiyas). In this type, the shared description between the original case and the new case is so predominant that the differing attribute is known to be irrelevant based on the established custom of the Law (ʿādat al-sharʿ). This principle allows analogy without needing a specific effective cause (ʿilla). The author uses the example of analogizing a female slave to a male slave in the ruling of partial emancipation, where gender is known to be irrelevant from the Law's consistent practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the shared description is numerous (not counted or detailed) and the differing description is only one attribute. For example, in the analogy between male and female slaves regarding partial emancipation, the shared aspects of slavery are predominant, and the difference is only gender.
2
Note the irrelevance of the differing attribute
Understand that the differing attribute (gender) is known to have no bearing on the ruling based on the custom of the Law. The Law consistently disregards gender in rulings related to slavery, as it disregards color, height, time, and place.
3
Distinguish from other types of analogy
Contrast this type with analogies based on a suggestive meaning (mukhayyal), effective cause (mu'thir), or explicit textual attachment. Here, none of those are established; the basis is purely the Law's habitual practice.
4
Apply the principle to new cases
In usul al-fiqh, when confronted with a new case, check if the shared attributes are predominant and any differing attribute is consistently disregarded by the Law. If so, the analogy is valid under this fourth type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن يكون ما فيه الاشتراك غير معدود ولا مفصل لأنه الأكثر، وما فيه الافتراق شيئاً واحداً ويعلم أن جنس المعنى الذي فيه الافتراق، لا مدخل له في هذا الحكم، مهما التفت إلى الشرع كقوله : من أعتق شخصاً له من عبد، قوم عليه الباقي فإنّا نقيس الأمةَ عليه لا لأنَّا عرفنا اجتماعهما في معنى مخيل أو مؤثر أو مضاف إليه الحكم بلفظه لأنه لم يبن لنا بعد المعنى المخيل فيه، ولا لأنا رأيناهما متقاربين فقط، فإنه لو وقع النظر في ولاية النكاح، وبان أن الأمةَ تُجبر على النكاح، فلا يتبين لنا أن العبد في معناه والقرب من الجانبين على وتيرة واحدة، ولكن إذا التفتنا إلى عادة الشرع علمنا قطعاً أنه ليس يتغير حكم الرق والعشق بالذكورة والأنوثة، كما لا يتغيّر بالسواد والبياض والطول والقصر والزمان والمكان وأمثالها .
The fourth: that what contains the commonality is not counted nor detailed because it is the majority, and what contains the separation is one thing, and it is known that the kind of meaning in which the separation occurs has no entry in this ruling, whenever consideration is given to the Law. For example, his saying: Whoever frees a share of his slave, the remainder is evaluated for him. So we analogize the female slave to him [the male slave] not because we know they share in a meaning that is suggestive (mukhayyal), effective (mu'thir), or attached to the ruling by its wording, for the suggestive meaning has not been established for us; nor because we see them only approximate, for if one considers the guardianship of marriage, and it is shown that the female slave is compelled to marry, it does not become clear to us that the male slave is in its meaning and the closeness is on one level. But when we look at the custom of the Law, we know with certainty that the ruling of slavery and [؟] does not change with masculinity and femininity, just as it does not change with blackness and whiteness, height and shortness, time and place, and the like.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth type, where the shared description is not counted or detailed because it is predominant, while the differing description is only one thing, and it is known that the kind of meaning involving the difference has no bearing on the ruling according to the Law. The author gives the example of a male slave being partially freed: the Hadith says that if someone frees a share of his slave, the rest of the slave's value is evaluated and the owner must pay. A female slave is analogized to a male slave in this ruling. The analogy is not based on a suggestive meaning (mukhayyal), effective meaning (mu'thir), or explicit textual attachment (idafah) because none of these are established. Nor is it based on mere approximation. The author points out that if one considers the guardianship of marriage, a female slave is compelled to marry, but that doesn't mean the male slave is similar. Rather, the basis is the custom of the Law (adah al-shar'), which indicates that rulings of slavery are not affected by gender, as they are not affected by color, height, time, place, etc. Since the difference (gender) is known to be irrelevant in this context, the analogy is valid. This type relies on a general principle derived from the Law's practice that certain attributes are consistently disregarded.

Key Terms
عَادَة الشَّرْع (ʿādat al-sharʿ)
مُخَيَّل (mukhayyal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of preponderance shows that analogy can be based on the habitual practice of the Law disregarding certain attributes, without requiring an explicit cause.
