The Material of Syllogism and the Layers of Expression
Author defines material and explains layers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four conditions that qualify knowledge as material for a syllogism and distinguish the three layers of expression from the true inner knowledge.

Core Idea
The true material of a syllogism is not words but certain inner knowledge—writing, speech, and inner speech are only husks.

In the previous section, the author discussed the form of the syllogism. Now he turns to its material. He argues that not any knowledge suffices; it must be assertoric, true, certain, and universal. Moreover, the real material is not the uttered premise but the inner knowledge it signifies. Follow his careful narrowing of conditions and his metaphor of layers (qushūr) to understand what truly grounds a syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matter-form distinction
The author analogizes a syllogism to a bed: matter is like wood, form is like the bed's shape. Confirm you understand this basic distinction before proceeding.
2
List the four conditions for material
Read the Arabic where the author narrows 'sciences' step by step: not every science, but assertoric (tasdiqi); not every assertoric, but true (sadiq); not every true, but certain (yaqini); not every certain, but universal certain (kulli yaqini). Write down each condition.
3
Understand why certainty is required
The author notes that something can be true in itself (e.g., known only to God) but not certain for the observer; such a proposition cannot serve as material for a syllogism seeking certainty. Reflect on an example: a hidden fact that is true but unknown cannot ground a valid argument.
4
Identify the three layers of expression
From the Arabic: the first layer is written script (suwar marquma), the second is spoken utterance (nutq), the third is inner speech (hadith al-nafs). Recognize that these are 'husks' (qushur) protecting the core meaning.
5
Locate the true material as the fourth degree
The author says the true material is the knowledge signified by the words, which is reached after the three layers. This is the fourth degree—the certain inner knowledge itself, not any of the expressive layers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني في مادة القياس قد ذكرنا أن كل مركب فهو متألف من شيئين : أحدهما : كالمادة الجارية منه مجرى الخشب من السرير ، والثاني : كالصورة الجارية منه مجرى صورة السرير من السرير، وقد تكلمنا على صورة القياس وتركيبه ووجوه تأليفه بما يقنع فلنتكلم في مادته، ومادته هي العلوم، لكن لا كل علم بل العلم التصديقي دون العلم التصوري، وإنما العلم التصوري مادة الحد والعلم التصديقي هو العلم بنسبة ذوات الحقائق بعضها إلى بعض بالإيجاب أو السلب ولا كل تصديقي بل التصديقي الصادق في نفسه، ولا كل صادق بل الصادق اليقيني . فرب شيء في نفسه صادق عند الله وليس يقينا عند الناظر، فلا يصلح أن يكون عنده مادة للقياس الذي يطلب به استنتاج اليقين، ولا كل يقيني بل اليقيني الكلي، أعني أنه يكون كذلك في كل حال. ومهما قلنا: مواد المقدمات القياس هي كان ذلك مجازا من وجه إذ المقدمة عبارة عن نطق باللسان والمادة الحقيقية هي العلم الذي لفظ الموضوع والمحمول دالان عليه لا اللفظ بل الموضوع والمحمول هي العلوم الثابتة في النفس دون الألفاظ، ولكن لا يمكن التفهيم إلا باللفظ ، والمادة الحقيقية هي التي تنتهي إليه في الدرجة الرابعة بعد ثلاثة قشور القشر الأول : هو الصور المرقومة بالكتابة . الثاني : هو النطق، فإنه الأصوات المرتبة التي هي مدلول الكتابة ودالة على الحديث الذي في النفس . الثالث : هو حديث النفس الذي هو علم بترتيب الحروف ونظم الكلام، إما منطوقا به وإما مكتوبا.
The second consideration: on the material of the syllogism. We have already mentioned that every composite is composed of two things: one is like the material, which runs the course of wood from a bed, and the second is like the form, which runs the course of the form of a bed from the bed. And we have spoken on the form of the syllogism, its composition, and the aspects of its arrangement sufficiently, so let us speak on its material. And its material is the sciences, but not every science, rather the assertoric science (al-ʿilm al-taṣdīqī), not the conceptual science (al-ʿilm al-taṣawwurī). For the conceptual science is the material of the definition, while the assertoric science is the knowledge of the relation of the essences of realities to each other by affirmation or negation. And not every assertoric, but the assertoric that is true in itself. And not every true, but the true that is certain. For sometimes a thing is true in itself with God but is not certain for the observer, so it is not suitable to be for him material for the syllogism by which certainty is sought. And not every certain, but the universal certain, meaning that it is so in every case. And whenever we say: the materials of the premises of the syllogism are such-and-such, that is metaphorically in a sense, for the premise is an expression by the tongue, and the true material is the knowledge which the word of subject and predicate signify, not the word. Rather the subject and predicate are the sciences established in the soul without the words. But instruction cannot be done except by the word. And the true material is that which ends in the fourth degree after three layers. The first layer: is the written forms. The second: is speech, for it is the ordered sounds that are the signification of writing and indicate the inner speech. The third: is inner speech (ḥadīth al-nafs), which is knowledge of the arrangement of letters and the organization of speech, either spoken or written.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage introduces the second part on the material (mādda) of the syllogism. The author first recalls the general principle that every composite has matter and form, analogizing to a bed (wood as matter, shape as form). Having covered the form of the syllogism (figures, moods, etc.), he now turns to the material. He defines material as the sciences (ʿulūm), but not all sciences: it must be assertoric knowledge (al-ʿilm al-taṣdīqī) which affirms or negates relations between essences, as opposed to conceptual knowledge (al-ʿilm al-taṣawwurī) which is the material of definitions. Then he narrows it further: not all assertoric knowledge, but that which is true in itself. And not all true, but that which is certain (yaqīnī). He notes that something can be true in itself (e.g., a true proposition known only to God) but not certain for the observer, so it cannot serve as material for a syllogism seeking certainty. Moreover, it must be universal certain (al-yaqīnī al-kullī), meaning applicable in every case. He then clarifies that when we speak of 'materials of premises', this is metaphorical, because the premise is a verbal utterance, whereas the true material is the knowledge signified by the words—the subject and predicate are the sciences established in the soul, not the words themselves. However, communication requires words. The true material is reached after three layers: (1) written script (suwar marqūma), (2) spoken utterance (nuṭq), (3) inner speech (ḥadīth al-nafs), which is the knowledge of arranging letters and organizing speech. The true material is the fourth degree—the knowledge that is signified, not the linguistic layers. This shows the priority of inner meaning over external wording and emphasizes that the logician must seek certain inner knowledge, not just verbal forms.

Key Terms
مادة القياس
علم تصديقي
يقيني
حديث النفس
قشور

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The material of a syllogism must be assertoric, true, certain, and universal knowledge, and its true locus is inner knowledge, not the outer husks of writing, speech, or inner speech.

Defining the Material of the Syllogism
Author defines the material of the syllogism as knowledge in the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the material of the syllogism as certain knowledge in the soul and distinguish it from syllogistic form.

Core Idea
The material of the syllogism is the certain knowledge in the soul, not the words or form.

The author opens the fourth part of his work by defining the core (al-lubab) as knowledge subsisting in the soul, an impression corresponding to the known. This knowledge is the actual content—the premises—that constitute the matter of the syllogism. Understanding this material is crucial because the validity and certainty of a syllogism depend on the quality of its premises, not just their arrangement.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of the material
Find where the author explicitly states 'these knowledges are the matter of the syllogism' and note his description of knowledge as an impression in the soul.
The term 'matter' (maddah) contrasts with 'form' (surah).
2
Identify what the material is not
The author warns that the material is not inner speech (hadith al-nafs) nor written words. Focus on his example of the writer and the wall.
Inner speech is a concomitant, not the essence.
3
Extract the essential characteristics
The material is 'true assenting knowledges' (al-‘ulum al-haqiqiyya al-tasdiqiyya) that, when arranged properly, prepare the soul for the conclusion by divine grace.
Certainty (yaqin) is the highest quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع وهو اللباب : هو العلم القائم بالنفس الذي حقيقته ترجع إلى انتقاش النفس بمثال مطابق للمعلوم؛ فهذه العلوم هي مواد القياس وعسر تجريدها في النفس دون نظم الألفاظ بحديث النفس لا ينبغي أن يخيل إليك الاتحاد بين العلم والحديث، فإن الكاتب أيضاً قد يعسر عليه تصوّر معنى إلا أن يتمثل له رقوم الكتابة الدالة على الشيء، حتى إذا تفكر في الجدار تصوّر عنده لفظ الجدار مكتوباً. ولكن لما كان العلم بالجدار غير موقوف على معرفة أصل الكتابة لم يشكل عليه أن هذا مقارن لازم للعلم لا عينه، وكذلك يتصوّر أن إنساناً يعلم علوماً كثيرة وهو لا يعرف اللغات فلا يكون في نفسه حديث نفس، أعني اشتغالاً بترتيب الألفاظ، فإذن العلوم الحقيقية التصديقية هي مواد القياس، فإنها إذا أحضرت في الذهن على ترتيب مخصوص استعدت النفس لأن يحدث فيها العلم بالنتيجة من عند الله تعالى، فإذن مهما قلنا مواد القياس المقدمات اليقينية فلا تفهم منه إلا ما ذكرناه . ثم كما أن صورة الاستدارة والنقش للدينار زائد على مادة الدينار، فإن المادة للدينار هي الذهب الإبريز، فكذا في القياس، وكما أن الذهب الذي هو الدينار له أربعة أحوال : أعلاها أن يكون ذهباً خالصاً إبريزاً لا غش فيه أصلاً. والثانية : أن يكون ذهباً مقارناً لا في غاية رتبته العليا، ولا كذلك الذهب الإبريز الخالص. والثالثة : أن يكون ذهباً كثير الغش لاختلاط النقرة والنحاس به . والرابعة : أن لا يكون ذهباً أصلاً بل يكون جنساً على حدة مشبهاً بالذهب
And the fourth, which is the core: it is knowledge subsisting in the soul, the reality of which reduces to the soul's being impressed with a form corresponding to the known. So these knowledges are the matter of the syllogism, and their abstraction in the soul without arrangement of words by inner speech is difficult. You should not imagine that knowledge and inner speech are identical, for the writer also may find it difficult to conceive a meaning unless the marks of writing that indicate the thing are represented to him, such that when he thinks of the wall, the written word 'wall' is pictured for him. But since knowledge of the wall is not dependent on knowing the origin of writing, it is not problematic for him that this is a concomitant accompaniment of knowledge, not its essence. Similarly, one can conceive a person knowing many sciences while not knowing languages, so there is no inner speech in his soul, meaning an occupation with arranging words. Thus, the true assenting knowledges are the matter of the syllogism. For when they are present in the mind in a specific arrangement, the soul becomes prepared for knowledge of the conclusion to occur in it from God Almighty. So whenever we say 'the matter of the syllogism' meaning certain premises, do not understand from it except what we have mentioned. Then just as the form of roundness and engraving on a coin is additional to the matter of the coin, for the matter of the coin is pure gold, so too in the syllogism. And just as gold which is the coin has four states: the highest is that it be pure gold with absolutely no alloy. The second: that it be gold close to but not at its highest rank, and not like pure gold. The third: that it be gold with much alloy due to mixture of silver and copper. The fourth: that it be not gold at all but rather a separate genus resembling gold.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the material of the syllogism. The author begins by identifying 'the core' (al-lubab) as knowledge subsisting in the soul, which is an impression corresponding to the known object. These knowledges are the matter (maddah) of the syllogism. He warns against confusing knowledge with inner speech (hadith al-nafs), giving the example of a writer who pictures the written word 'wall' when thinking of the wall. This is a necessary concomitant, not the essence. He then asserts that true assenting knowledges are the material, and when arranged properly, the soul becomes prepared for the conclusion by divine grace. The author draws an analogy from coin-making: the form (roundness, engraving) is added to the matter (gold). Similarly, the syllogistic form is added to premises. Gold has four states: pure, near-pure, alloyed, and not gold at all but resembling it. This sets up the classification of syllogisms based on the quality of their premises.

Knowledge vs. Inner Speech
Author distinguishes knowledge from inner speech with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between knowledge (ilm) and inner speech (hadith al-nafs) using the author's arguments and examples.

Core Idea
Inner speech is a necessary concomitant of knowledge, but not identical to it.

The author warns against a common confusion: thinking that knowledge and inner speech are identical. He provides the example of a writer who cannot conceive a meaning without the written word, and the case of a mute person who knows many sciences without any inner speech. This distinction is crucial because it clarifies that the material of the syllogism is the knowledge itself, not its verbal expression.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's warning
Find the sentence where he says 'do not imagine that knowledge and inner speech are identical' and note his emphasis on their distinction.
The Arabic: 'لا ينبغي أن يخيل إليك الاتحاد بين العلم والحديث'.
2
Analyze the writer example
The writer pictures the written word 'wall' when thinking of the wall. The author says the written word is a necessary accompaniment, not the essence of knowledge.
This shows inner speech can aid conception but is not the same as knowledge.
3
Consider the mute scholar example
The author imagines a person knowing many sciences without knowing any languages, thus having no inner speech. This proves knowledge can exist without inner speech.
This demonstrates that inner speech is not essential to knowledge.

The Gold Analogy and Four States of Premises
Author uses gold states to classify syllogistic premises by quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify syllogistic premises according to the four states of gold described in the author's analogy.

Core Idea
Just as gold has four states of purity, premises have four levels: certain, near-certain, opinionative, and counterfeit.

The author uses a powerful analogy from coin-making: the form (roundness and engraving) is added to the matter (pure gold). Similarly, syllogistic form is added to premises. He then describes four states of gold that correspond to the quality of premises. Understanding this analogy helps us evaluate the epistemic strength of syllogisms based on their material.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy structure
The form of the coin (roundness, engraving) is additional to the matter (pure gold). In the syllogism, the form (figure and mood) is additional to the premises.
The matter of the coin is the substance; the matter of the syllogism is the premises.
2
Identify the four states of gold
List the four: (1) pure gold with no alloy, (2) gold close to pure but not highest, (3) gold with much alloy (silver/copper), (4) not gold at all but resembling it.
These correspond to four types of premises: yaqini (certain), qarib min al-yaqin (near-certain), al-mukhtalitah (mixed/opinionative), and al-batilah (false/resembling truth).
3
Map each gold state to a category of premises
Associate each gold state with a degree of certainty: pure = absolutely certain, near-pure = widely accepted but not certain, alloyed = probable but weak, counterfeit = completely invalid.
This mapping is a pedagogical interpretation; the author does not name these categories explicitly in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Meanings of Essential and Primary
Author defines two senses each for essential and primary with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of 'essential' (dhati) and two senses of 'primary' (awwali) and identify examples of each in logical and Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Essential and primary each have two distinct meanings; confusion leads to flawed demonstrations.

The foundational terms of logic often carry multiple technical meanings. In this passage, the author clarifies that 'essential' (al-dhati) and 'primary' (al-awwali) each have two distinct senses. Understanding these nuances is crucial for properly evaluating definitions and self-evident truths in Islamic scholarly discourse. This lesson will guide you through the distinctions and their applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of dhati
First sense: the predicate is taken in the definition of the subject (e.g., 'animal' in 'man'). Second sense: the subject is taken in the definition of the predicate (e.g., 'nose' in 'snubness'). If neither is in the other's definition, they are not essential.
2
Apply the test for dhati
Check definitional inclusion. For 'man is rational': is 'rational' in the definition of 'man'? Yes, it is the differentia. So first sense. For 'snubness in nose': nose is in the definition of snubness, so second sense.
3
Identify the two senses of awwali
First sense: self-evident, requiring no middle term (e.g., 'two is greater than one'). Second sense: the predicate cannot be affirmed or denied of anything more general than the subject (e.g., 'falls ill' cannot be said of a genus beyond animal without losing truth).
4
Apply the test for awwali
Check if a proposition is self-evident (first sense) or if the predicate applies to a wider genus (second sense). For 'man falls ill', 'falls ill' applies to animal (more general), so it is not primary for man in the second sense; it is primary for animal because it does not apply to body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان معنى الذاتي والأولي أما الذاتي فيطلق على وجهين : أحدهما: أن يكون المحمول مأخوذاً في حد الموضوع مقوماً له داخلاً في حقيقته كقولنا : الإنسان حيوان فيقال : الحيوان ذاتي للإنسان أي هو مقوم له كما سبق بيانه. وإما أن يكون الموضوع مأخوذاً في حد المحمول كقولنا: بعض الحيوان إنسان فإن المحمول هو الإنسان ههنا لا الحيوان والإنسان لا يؤخذ في حد الحيوان بل الحيوان يؤخذ في حد الإنسان فكل شيئين لا يؤخذ أحدهما في حد الآخر فليس أحدهما ذاتياً للآخر. وقد يمثل بالفطوسة في الأنف فإنه ذاتي للأنف بالمعنى الأخير، إذ لا يمكن تحديد الفطوسة إلا بذكر الأنف في حده. وأما الأولي : فإنه يقال أيضاً على وجهين: أحدهما ما هو أولي في العقل أي لا يحتاج في معرفته إلى وسط كقولنا : الاثنان أكثر من الواحد. والثاني أن يكون بحيث لا يمكن إيجاب المحمول أو سلبه على معنى آخر أعم من الموضوع . فإذا قلنا : الإنسان يمرض ويصح ، لم يكن أولياً له بهذا المعنى إذ يقال على ما هو أعم منه وهو الحيوان : نعم هو للحيوان أولى، لأنه لا يقال على ما هو أعم . الجسم ؛ وكذلك قبول الانتقال للحيوان ليس بأولي إذ يقال على ما هو أعم وهو الجسم، فإنه لو ارتفع الحيوان بقي قبول الانتقال ولو ارتفع الجسم لم يبق.
Chapter on clarifying the meaning of essential and primary. As for essential, it is used in two ways. One of them: that the predicate is taken in the definition of the subject, constituting it, entering into its reality, like our saying: 'Man is an animal'; so it is said: 'Animal is essential for man', meaning it is constitutive of him, as previously explained. Or that the subject is taken in the definition of the predicate, like our saying: 'Some animal is a man'; for the predicate here is 'man', not 'animal', and 'man' is not taken in the definition of 'animal', but rather 'animal' is taken in the definition of 'man'. So whichever two things, one of them is not taken in the definition of the other, then one of them is not essential for the other. And it may be exemplified by 'snubness' in the nose, for it is essential for the nose in the latter sense, since it is impossible to define snubness except by mentioning the nose in its definition. As for primary, it is also said in two ways. One of them: what is primary in the intellect, i.e., does not require a middle term for its knowledge, like our saying: 'Two is greater than one.' The second: that it be such that it is impossible to affirm or deny the predicate of another meaning more general than the subject. So if we say: 'Man falls ill and recovers', it is not primary for him in this sense, since it is said of what is more general than him, namely animal. Yes, it is primary for animal, because it is not said of what is more general—namely body. Similarly, the capacity for movement for animal is not primary, since it is said of what is more general, namely body, for if animal were removed, the capacity for movement would remain, but if body were removed, it would not remain.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines two terms: 'essential' (الذاتي) and 'primary' (الأولي), each with two distinct meanings. For 'essential', the first meaning is that the predicate is part of the subject's definition (e.g., 'animal' is essential to 'man' because it is in man's definition). The second meaning reverses the relation: the subject is part of the predicate's definition (e.g., 'snubness' is essential to 'nose' because nose is part of snubness's definition). The author warns that if neither is part of the other's definition, then neither is essential. For 'primary', the first meaning is self-evident truths requiring no middle term (e.g., 'two is greater than one'). The second meaning is that the predicate cannot be affirmed or denied of anything more general than the subject. For example, 'falls ill' can be said of animal (more general than man), so it is not primary for man; but for animal it is primary because it cannot be said of body (which is more general than animal). The author uses capacity for movement to illustrate: it applies to body, so it is not primary for animal. This careful distinction is crucial for assessing demonstrations and definitions.

Key Terms
ذاتي
أولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if 'capacity for movement' (قبول الانتقال) can be said of both animal and body, is it primary for animal in the second sense?
A) Yes, because it is essential for animal.
B) No, because it is said of something more general (body).
C) Yes, because it is self-evident.
D) No, because it is a differentia.

Key Takeaway
Essential and primary each have two strict relational meanings: definitional inclusion for essential, and self-evidence or non-applicability to a wider genus for primary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply the two senses of 'essential' to the pair 'prayer' and 'wudu'. Is 'wudu' essential for 'prayer'? Justify your answer using the passage's criteria.
?

Why Intrinsic Cannot Be Sought
Author explains why the intrinsic cannot be sought when essence is known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between seeking an intrinsic attribute versus an accidental attribute in a question about essence.

Core Idea
Intrinsic attributes are known with the essence; only accidents are sought.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: if the soul's substantiality is intrinsic, why do we seek to know whether the soul is a substance? The answer reveals a crucial distinction between knowing an essence and knowing an accidental attribute. This distinction is foundational for understanding what can be the object of inquiry in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
The objector asks: Why say intrinsic cannot be sought, when we seek knowledge of the soul's substantiality? Locate the objection in the Arabic: 'فإن قيل : فلم قلتم إن الذاتي بالمعنى الأول لا يكون مطلوباً...'
The objection uses the example of the soul to challenge the claim.
2
Identify the respondent's position (mudda'i)
The author responds that if we truly know the soul, its substantiality is already known. So seeking it is redundant.
The key phrase: 'من عرف النفس لم يتصوّر منه طلب كونه جوهراً'
3
Analyze the distinction
The author distinguishes between knowing the essence fully vs. knowing only an accidental attribute (like being a mover/perceiver). When we ask about the soul's substance, we only have an accidental property, not the essence.
This is the core logical move: the object of inquiry is not the intrinsic itself but the genus of the thing that possesses the accident.
4
Apply the rule
The general rule: an intrinsic (dhati) of a known essence is not sought; but if we only know the name or image, we can seek the genus. This clarifies the limits of inquiry.
The final sentence: 'فأما على غير هذا الوجه فلا يمكن'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فلم قلتم إن الذاتي بالمعنى الأول لا يكون مطلوباً، ونحن نطلب العلم بأن النفس جوهر أم لا، والجوهرية للنفس ذاتية إذ من عرف النفس فيعرف كونه جوهراً إن كان جوهراً؟ قلنا : من عرف النفس لم يتصوّر منه طلب كونه جوهراً، إذ معرفة جوهريته سابقة على المعرفة به، لكنّا إذا طلبنا أن النفس جوهر أم لا لم نكن عرفنا من النفس إلا أمراً عارضاً له وهو المحرك والمدرك، ويكون ذلك مثل الأبيض للثلج، والمطلوب جنس المعروض له وهو غير مقوم لماهية العارض أعني الجوهرية ليس مقوماً للمدرك والمحرك تقويم الذاتيات، وكذلك كلّما حصل عندنا خياله أو اسمه لا حقيقته، أمكن أن نطلب جنس ذلك الذي حصل لنا اسمه أو خياله، فأما على غير هذا الوجه فلا يمكن .
If it is said: Why do you say that the intrinsic in the first sense cannot be sought, while we seek knowledge of whether the soul is a substance or not, and substantiality is intrinsic to the soul, since whoever knows the soul knows it to be a substance if it is a substance? We reply: Whoever knows the soul is not imagined to seek its substantiality, because knowledge of its substantiality precedes knowledge of it. However, when we ask whether the soul is a substance or not, we have only known of the soul an accidental attribute, namely that it moves and perceives, and that is like whiteness for snow. What is sought is the genus of that which possesses the accident, and it is not constitutive of the essence of the accident; I mean substantiality is not constitutive of the mover and perceiver as intrinsic constituents are. Likewise, whenever we have its image or its name but not its reality, we can seek the genus of that of which we have the name or image. But in any other way, this is not possible.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a definitional response to an objection. The author clarifies why the intrinsic attribute cannot be the object of inquiry when the essence is already known. The objection raises the example of the soul: we seek to know if the soul is a substance, yet substantiality is intrinsic to the soul. The author responds by distinguishing between knowing the essence and knowing an accidental attribute. If we truly know the soul, its substantiality is already known. But when we inquire, we often only know an accidental property (like moving and perceiving) and seek the genus of that which possesses the accident. The intrinsic (substantiality) is not sought because it is already known with the essence. This mirrors the example of snow: whiteness is accidental, but the genus of snow is sought when only its name or image is known. Thus, the intrinsic is not sought unless there is ignorance of the essence itself.

مطلوب
جوهر

A) Its full essence
B) Only an accidental attribute
C) Its genus
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
An intrinsic attribute cannot be sought because it is already known with the essence; what is sought is the genus of an accidental attribute when the essence is unknown.

The First Two Causes: Efficient and Material
Ghazali begins enumeration of the four meanings of 'illa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the efficient cause from the material cause as defined by Ghazali and identify them in examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The term 'illa has multiple senses; conflating efficient and material causes leads to confusion in metaphysics and fiqh.

Al-Ghazali opens his discussion on causation by stating that 'illa (cause) is used in four distinct senses. In this passage, he introduces the first two: the efficient cause (that from which motion arises) and the material cause (that without which existence is impossible). Understanding these basic categories is essential for correctly interpreting later discussions of causality in kalam, falsafa, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being explained
Notice that the passage begins by defining the term 'illa (cause) and then enumerates its meanings. Your task is to isolate each meaning as a distinct concept.
2
Extract the first meaning: efficient cause
Read the first meaning: 'that from which motion arises essentially'. This is the efficient cause (al-fa'il), the agent that produces the effect. Ghazali gives examples: the carpenter is the cause of the chair, the father is the cause of the child.
In Islamic theology, the efficient cause is ultimately Allah, but secondary causes like the carpenter are real causes in the ordinary sense.
3
Extract the second meaning: material cause
Read the second meaning: 'the matter and that without which existence is not possible'. This is the material cause (al-madda), the substrate or stuff from which something is made. Though Ghazali does not give explicit examples here, the typical examples are wood for the chair and sperm for the child.
The material cause is passive; it receives the action of the efficient cause.
4
Compare the two causes
Notice the key difference: the efficient cause is active (it initiates motion or change), while the material cause is passive (it is the subject of change). Memorize the examples to anchor the distinction.
5
Prepare for the remaining causes
Ghazali mentions that there are four meanings in total, but the text cuts off. The next two are the formal cause (the essence) and the final cause (the purpose). Anticipate how they differ from the efficient and material causes.
This lesson only covers the first two; later lessons will complete the enumeration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أقسام العلة العلة تطلق على أربعة معانٍ : الأول : ما منه بذاته الحركة وهو السبب في وجود الشيء كالنجار للكرسي والأب للصبي. الثاني: المادة وما لا بد من وجوده
Section on the divisions of cause. The term 'cause' is applied to four meanings: First: that from which the movement arises essentially, and it is the reason for the existence of a thing, like the carpenter for the chair and the father for the child. Second: the matter and that without which existence is not possible.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section where al-Ghazali begins to enumerate the four Aristotelian causes. He states that 'illa (cause) is used in four senses. The first is the efficient cause (al-fa‘il), which is that from which motion proceeds essentially and is the reason for the thing's existence. He gives examples: the carpenter is the cause of the chair, and the father is the cause of the child. The second is the material cause (al-madda), which is that without which the thing cannot exist, such as wood for the chair or the sperm for the child. The text is cut off at this point, so the remaining two causes (formal and final) are not present. This passage is a parsing exercise: the student must recognize these as the first two of the four causes and understand their definitions and examples.

Key Terms
علة (illa): Ghazali defines 'illa as having four meanings; here he gives the first two: the efficient cause (that from which motion arises) and the material cause (that without which existence is impossible). [Note: In later logic, 'illa often refers to the middle term or the reason in a syllogism; here it is used in the broader philosophical sense of causation.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) That from which motion originates essentially, like the carpenter for the chair
C) The end or purpose of a thing
D) The form or essence of a thing

Key Takeaway
Ghazali distinguishes the efficient cause (active source of motion) from the material cause (passive substrate), forming the first two of the four Aristotelian causes.

The Four Causes in Juristic Terminology
Mapping the four causes to fiqhi terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map the four Aristotelian causes (material, efficient, formal, final) to their juristic equivalents (mahall, ahl, sura, hukm) using the passage's examples.

Core Idea
Jurists renamed the four causes to fit fiqh: material becomes mahall, efficient becomes ahl, final becomes hukm, form remains sura.

This passage explains how the logical framework of the four causes is adapted in Islamic jurisprudence. Understanding this mapping is essential because a legal ruling's validity depends on correctly identifying its material, agent, form, and purpose. The author uses marriage and sale as clear examples to show how all four must be present for a valid legal act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four Aristotelian causes
Recall the four causes: material (what it is made of), efficient (what makes it), formal (its structure), and final (its purpose). In the passage, the author first lists these and then gives their juristic names.
The passage starts: 'The names of the four causes in the language of the jurists...'
2
Find the juristic equivalent for each cause
The author explicitly maps: material cause = mahall (place/subject matter, e.g., vulva in marriage, wood in carpentry), efficient cause = ahl (agent, e.g., husband or carpenter), final cause = hukm (ruling, e.g., permissibility in marriage), formal cause = sura (form, e.g., the contract formula or the shape of a bed).
Note that the formal cause is the only one that retains its name (sura) in fiqh.
3
Analyze the marriage example
In marriage: husband (ahl) is efficient, vulva (mahall) is material, permissibility (hukm) is final, contract formula (sura) is formal. The author emphasizes that unless all four are present, the marriage does not exist (e.g., without a valid contract, there is no marriage; without permissibility, it is void).
Similarly for sale: seller (ahl) and sold item (mahall) are not enough without ownership (hukm) and contract (sura).
4
Check the necessity of the final cause
The passage states that the final cause (ghaya) is indispensable: it must be a motivating reason before the act exists, and it must be actual after the act exists. Without the final purpose, the act lacks validity.
For example, a marriage that does not produce permissibility (halal relationship) is considered non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الرابع : في لواحق القياس. أسماء العلل الأربع في لغة الفقهاء. وكذا في الأحكام الفقهية، والفقهاء ربما سمّوا المادة محلاً والفاعل الذي هو كالنجار والأب أهلاً، والغاية حكماً، فإذا فرض النكاح فالزوج أهل والبضع محلّ والحلّ غاية وصيغة العقد كأنها الصورة وما لم تجتمع هذه الأمور لا يتمّ للنكاح وجود، ولذلك قيل : النكاح الذي لا يفيد الحلّ لا وجود له، وكذا البيع الذي لا يفيد الملك. فإن وجود الغاية لا بد منه وكونها معقولاً باعثاً شرط قبل الوجود، وكونها موجودة بالفعل واجب بعد الوجود ومهما قدّر الفاعل والمادة موجود موجوداً لم يلزم وجود الشيء في كل حال كالنجار والخشب والأب والنطفة والبايع والمبيع، ومهما وجدت الصورة لزم وجود الشيء كصورة السرير وصورة الإنسانية، ومهما وجدت الغاية بالفعل لزم وجود الشيء كالحلّ في النكاح والصلوح للاكتنان والجلوس في البيت، والشيء بهذه الجهات الأربع يختلف في هذا المعنى. ثم كلّ هذه العلل إما بعيدة كإسلام المرأة للزوج عند ملك الزوج نصف الصداق، إنه علّة الصداق والصداق هو العلّة القريبة للتسليم، وإما بالقوة كالإسكار للخمر قبل الشرب، وإما الفعل كما في حال الشرب، وإما خاصة كالزنا للرجم، وإما عامة كالجناية للرجم أو العقوبة، وإما بالذات وهو المُسمّى علة عند الفقهاء كالزنا للرجم وإما بالعرض كالإحصان له وهو الذي يُسمّى شرطاً، فإن الرجم لا يجب إلا بالإحصان، وهي خصال كمال ولكن يعمل عمل العلة عنده، كما لو أرسلت الدعامة من تحت السقف فنزل فيقال نزوله بعلة الثقل، ولكن عند إشالة الدعامة فإن للهوي شرطاً، وهو فراغ جهة الأسفل عن جسم صلب لا ينخرق. وأمثلة هذا في المعقولات كثيرة، فلذلك اقتصرنا على الأمثلة الفقهية، والمقصود أن المعلل في الفقه والمعقول إذا توجهت المطالبة عليه بالعلة، ينبغي أن يذكر العلة الخاصة القريبة التي بالفعل حتى تقطع المطالبة بلم، وإلا فيكون الطلب قائماً.
The fourth consideration: on the concomitants of the syllogism. The names of the four causes in the language of the jurists. And likewise in juristic rulings, and the jurists sometimes call the material a 'place' (mahall), and the efficient which is like the carpenter and father a 'person' (ahl), and the final a 'ruling' (hukm). So if marriage is assumed, the husband is the efficient, the vulva is the material, the permissibility is the final, and the formula of the contract is as if it were the form; and unless these matters are combined, the existence of marriage is not completed. Therefore it was said: A marriage that does not bring about permissibility has no existence, and likewise a sale that does not bring about ownership. For the existence of the final is indispensable: its being a rational motivator is a condition before existence, and its being actual existence is necessary after existence. Whenever the efficient and the material are assumed to exist, the existence of the thing does not necessarily follow in every case—like the carpenter and wood, the father and the sperm drop, the seller and the sold item. And whenever the form exists, the existence of the thing necessarily follows—like the form of a bed and the form of humanity. And whenever the final exists in actuality, the existence of the thing necessarily follows—like permissibility in marriage and suitability for concealment and sitting in the house. And the thing differs in this meaning according to these four aspects. Then all of these causes are either remote—like the woman's conversion to Islam for the husband when the husband owns half the dowry: it is a cause of the dowry, and the dowry is the proximate cause of delivery—or potential—like intoxication for wine before drinking—or actual—as in the state of drinking—or specific—like adultery for stoning—or general—like criminality for stoning or punishment—or essential—which is called a cause by the jurists, like adultery for stoning—or accidental—like being married (ihsan) for it, which is called a condition, for stoning is not obligatory except with being married, and it is a complete characteristic, but it acts like a cause according to him, just as if a prop is removed from under a ceiling and it falls, it is said its fall is due to the cause of weight, but when the prop is lifted, the fall has a condition, namely the emption of the lower direction from a solid body that does not break. Examples of this in intelligibles are many, therefore we confined ourselves to juristic examples. And the point is that the one who is asked for the cause in law and in logic, when a demand is made upon him for the cause, he should mention the specific, proximate, actual cause so that the questioning with 'why?' is cut off, otherwise the demand remains.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines the four causes as used by jurists in the context of legal reasoning, drawing an analogy to logical causes (the 'concomitants of the syllogism'). The author first renames the four Aristotelian causes—material, efficient, formal, final—into juristic terms: material is called 'mahall' (subject matter), efficient is 'ahl' (agent), final is 'hukm' (ruling), and form is 'sura' (form). The example of marriage illustrates: the husband (agent), the vulva (material), the permissibility (final), and the contract formula (form) must all be present; otherwise, marriage has no existence. Similarly, a sale without ownership is void. The author then explains the necessity of the final cause: its existence as a motivator is a condition before the act, and its actual existence is necessary after the act. The mere presence of efficient and material does not guarantee existence (like carpenter and wood alone do not produce a bed), but the form and final cause do. The passage then categorizes causes along several axes: remote vs proximate, potential vs actual, specific vs general, essential vs accidental. Essential cause is what jurists simply call 'cause' (like adultery for stoning), while accidental (like being married for stoning) is called a 'condition'. The author gives a concrete analogy: a falling ceiling is caused by weight, but the removal of the prop is a condition. The goal is to clarify that when asked for the cause of a legal or logical effect, one should mention the specific, proximate, actual cause, otherwise the inquiry persists.

Key Takeaway
The four causes in fiqh are mahall, ahl, sura, and hukm; all must be present for a valid legal act, and the final cause is especially necessary.

Categories of Causes: Essential vs. Accidental in Fiqh
Classifying causes and distinguishing essential from accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an essential cause (illa bi-dh-dhat) and an accidental condition (shart) using the passage's criteria and examples.

Core Idea
Only the specific, proximate, actual cause should be cited when asked for the 'why' in fiqh; conditions are not true causes.

The passage not only names the four causes but also classifies them along several axes: remote/proximate, potential/actual, specific/general, and most importantly essential/accidental. Understanding this classification is crucial because a jurist must give the correct 'illa' when challenged in legal reasoning. The example of stoning illustrates how adultery is the essential cause while being married (ihsan) is merely a condition.

Guided Path
1
List the pairs of cause categories
The passage mentions five dichotomies: remote vs proximate, potential vs actual, specific vs general, essential vs accidental (bi-dh-dhat vs bi-l-‘arad). For each pair, the author gives a fiqhi example.
Remote: woman's conversion to Islam for dowry; proximate: the dowry itself. Potential: intoxication before drinking; actual: intoxication during drinking. Specific: adultery for stoning; general: criminality for punishment. Essential: adultery for stoning; accidental: ihsan (being married) for stoning.
2
Focus on the essential vs accidental distinction
The author states that essential cause (bi-dh-dhat) is what jurists normally call 'illa', e.g., adultery causes stoning. Accidental cause (bi-l-‘arad) is called a condition (shart), e.g., ihsan (being married) is a condition for stoning, not a cause.
The passage says: 'and accidental like being married (ihsan) for it, which is called a condition'.
3
Analyze the ceiling example
The author gives an analogy: a ceiling falls when a prop is removed. The cause of falling is weight, but the removal of the prop is a condition. Similarly, in stoning, adultery is the cause, but being married is a condition that must be present for the cause to produce the effect.
This analogy clarifies that conditions are external factors that enable the cause to act, but are not the cause themselves.
4
Apply the rule: when asked for the cause, give the specific, proximate, actual cause
The passage concludes: if someone demands the reason for a ruling (with 'why?'), you must mention the cause that is specific (appropriate only to this ruling), proximate (not remote), and actual (not potential). Otherwise, the questioning continues.
For example, if asked 'why is this person stoned?', the answer should be 'because of adultery' (specific, proximate, actual), not 'because of criminality' (general) or 'because he is married' (accidental/condition).

النظر الرابع : في لواحق القياس. أسماء العلل الأربع في لغة الفقهاء. وكذا في الأحكام الفقهية، والفقهاء ربما سمّوا المادة محلاً والفاعل الذي هو كالنجار والأب أهلاً، والغاية حكماً، فإذا فرض النكاح فالزوج أهل والبضع محلّ والحلّ غاية وصيغة العقد كأنها الصورة وما لم تجتمع هذه الأمور لا يتمّ للنكاح وجود، ولذلك قيل : النكاح الذي لا يفيد الحلّ لا وجود له، وكذا البيع الذي لا يفيد الملك. فإن وجود الغاية لا بد منه وكونها معقولاً باعثاً شرط قبل الوجود، وكونها موجودة بالفعل واجب بعد الوجود ومهما قدّر الفاعل والمادة موجود موجوداً لم يلزم وجود الشيء في كل حال كالنجار والخشب والأب والنطفة والبايع والمبيع، ومهما وجدت الصورة لزم وجود الشيء كصورة السرير وصورة الإنسانية، ومهما وجدت الغاية بالفعل لزم وجود الشيء كالحلّ في النكاح والصلوح للاكتنان والجلوس في البيت، والشيء بهذه الجهات الأربع يختلف في هذا المعنى. ثم كلّ هذه العلل إما بعيدة كإسلام المرأة للزوج عند ملك الزوج نصف الصداق، إنه علّة الصداق والصداق هو العلّة القريبة للتسليم، وإما بالقوة كالإسكار للخمر قبل الشرب، وإما الفعل كما في حال الشرب، وإما خاصة كالزنا للرجم، وإما عامة كالجناية للرجم أو العقوبة، وإما بالذات وهو المُسمّى علة عند الفقهاء كالزنا للرجم وإما بالعرض كالإحصان له وهو الذي يُسمّى شرطاً، فإن الرجم لا يجب إلا بالإحصان، وهي خصال كمال ولكن يعمل عمل العلة عنده، كما لو أرسلت الدعامة من تحت السقف فنزل فيقال نزوله بعلة الثقل، ولكن عند إشالة الدعامة فإن للهوي شرطاً، وهو فراغ جهة الأسفل عن جسم صلب لا ينخرق. وأمثلة هذا في المعقولات كثيرة، فلذلك اقتصرنا على الأمثلة الفقهية، والمقصود أن المعلل في الفقه والمعقول إذا توجهت المطالبة عليه بالعلة، ينبغي أن يذكر العلة الخاصة القريبة التي بالفعل حتى تقطع المطالبة بلم، وإلا فيكون الطلب قائماً.
The fourth consideration: on the concomitants of the syllogism. The names of the four causes in the language of the jurists. And likewise in juristic rulings, and the jurists sometimes call the material a 'place' (mahall), and the efficient which is like the carpenter and father a 'person' (ahl), and the final a 'ruling' (hukm). So if marriage is assumed, the husband is the efficient, the vulva is the material, the permissibility is the final, and the formula of the contract is as if it were the form; and unless these matters are combined, the existence of marriage is not completed. Therefore it was said: A marriage that does not bring about permissibility has no existence, and likewise a sale that does not bring about ownership. For the existence of the final is indispensable: its being a rational motivator is a condition before existence, and its being actual existence is necessary after existence. Whenever the efficient and the material are assumed to exist, the existence of the thing does not necessarily follow in every case—like the carpenter and wood, the father and the sperm drop, the seller and the sold item. And whenever the form exists, the existence of the thing necessarily follows—like the form of a bed and the form of humanity. And whenever the final exists in actuality, the existence of the thing necessarily follows—like permissibility in marriage and suitability for concealment and sitting in the house. And the thing differs in this meaning according to these four aspects. Then all of these causes are either remote—like the woman's conversion to Islam for the husband when the husband owns half the dowry: it is a cause of the dowry, and the dowry is the proximate cause of delivery—or potential—like intoxication for wine before drinking—or actual—as in the state of drinking—or specific—like adultery for stoning—or general—like criminality for stoning or punishment—or essential—which is called a cause by the jurists, like adultery for stoning—or accidental—like being married (ihsan) for it, which is called a condition, for stoning is not obligatory except with being married, and it is a complete characteristic, but it acts like a cause according to him, just as if a prop is removed from under a ceiling and it falls, it is said its fall is due to the cause of weight, but when the prop is lifted, the fall has a condition, namely the emption of the lower direction from a solid body that does not break. Examples of this in intelligibles are many, therefore we confined ourselves to juristic examples. And the point is that the one who is asked for the cause in law and in logic, when a demand is made upon him for the cause, he should mention the specific, proximate, actual cause so that the questioning with 'why?' is cut off, otherwise the demand remains.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines the four causes as used by jurists in the context of legal reasoning, drawing an analogy to logical causes (the 'concomitants of the syllogism'). The author first renames the four Aristotelian causes—material, efficient, formal, final—into juristic terms: material is called 'mahall' (subject matter), efficient is 'ahl' (agent), final is 'hukm' (ruling), and form is 'sura' (form). The example of marriage illustrates: the husband (agent), the vulva (material), the permissibility (final), and the contract formula (form) must all be present; otherwise, marriage has no existence. Similarly, a sale without ownership is void. The author then explains the necessity of the final cause: its existence as a motivator is a condition before the act, and its actual existence is necessary after the act. The mere presence of efficient and material does not guarantee existence (like carpenter and wood alone do not produce a bed), but the form and final cause do. The passage then categorizes causes along several axes: remote vs proximate, potential vs actual, specific vs general, essential vs accidental. Essential cause is what jurists simply call 'cause' (like adultery for stoning), while accidental (like being married for stoning) is called a 'condition'. The author gives a concrete analogy: a falling ceiling is caused by weight, but the removal of the prop is a condition. The goal is to clarify that when asked for the cause of a legal or logical effect, one should mention the specific, proximate, actual cause, otherwise the inquiry persists.

The Two-Fold Division of the Book of Definition
Author introduces the two-fold division of the chapter on definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the chapter on definitions is structured into universal laws and detailed definitions, and anticipate the purpose of each part.

Core Idea
Definitions in logic are built on universal rules before diving into specific cases.

This passage opens the chapter on definitions (al-hadd). The author announces that the investigation will be divided into two arts (fanan). This two-part structure is typical in Islamic textbooks: first lay down the general principles (al-qawanin al-kulliya), then illustrate with concrete detailed definitions (al-hudud al-mufassala). Understanding this division helps you navigate the rest of the chapter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arts
Locate the phrase 'فنان' (two arts) in the Arabic. What are the two arts named?
The first is about universal laws, the second about detailed definitions.
2
Distinguish the scope
The first art deals with 'ما يجري من الحد مجرى القوانين الكلية' — that which runs in the manner of universal laws. The second deals with 'الحدود المفصلة' — detailed definitions. What is the difference in generality?
Universal laws are general rules; detailed definitions are specific applications.
3
Consider the pedagogical logic
Why put universal laws first? Because specific definitions only make sense in light of the rules that govern them.
This mirrors the structure of many Islamic sciences.
4
Relate to other Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, general principles (al-qawa'id al-usuliyya) are studied before specific cases (al-furu'). Similarly, in logic, the theory of definition precedes its application.

The Book of Definition. And the investigation in this book is encompassed by two arts: the first is concerning what runs from the definition in the manner of universal laws; and the second is concerning detailed definitions.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on definitions (al-hadd) by stating that the content is divided into two main parts, called 'two arts' (fanan). The first part deals with definitions in a broad, law-like sense—universal principles that govern how definitions are formed and evaluated. The second part delves into specific, detailed definitions (al-hudud al-mufassala), likely providing concrete examples and applications. This division sets up the structure for the remainder of the chapter, where the author will first establish the general rules of definition and then examine particular instances. It reflects a standard pedagogical approach in Islamic logic: first present the theoretical framework, then illustrate with examples.

Hadd and Rasm

Hadd and Rasm: Definitions that Capture Essence vs. Distinguishing Marks
Author explains two kinds of knowledge and defines hadd and rasm with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a hadd (definition) and a rasm (description) by identifying essential attributes (dhatiyyat) vs. accidental properties (awarid/khawass) used in each, and verify the convertibility condition.

Core Idea
Hadd captures essence; rasm captures distinguishing features without essence.

Ghazali opens his work on logic by reiterating the two kinds of knowledge – tasawwur (conceptualization) and tasdiq (assent) – and then introduces the tools needed to achieve complete conceptualization: genuine definition (hadd) and description (rasm). In this passage, he defines both terms with vivid examples from wine and human, showing how each yields a distinct kind of understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of knowledge
Find where Ghazali divides knowledge into tasawwur (knowledge of essences) and tasdiq (knowledge of relations). Note that complete tasawwur is attained via hadd, while tasdiq is attained via proof (hujja).
2
Distinguish summary vs. detailed understanding
Ghazali says the universal can be understood either summarily (jumli) through a mere name, or in a detailed summed-up way (mufassal) that enumerates all essential attributes (dhatiyyat). The latter is hadd. Check the examples: 'intoxicating drink pressed from grapes' (wine) and 'rational animal possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will, nourished' (human).
The detailed understanding is convertible with the name – i.e., the definition can replace the name.
3
Extract the essential attributes in the hadd examples
For wine: genus = drink, differentia = intoxicating & pressed from grapes. For human: genus = animal, differentia = rational, and additional essential properties like 'possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will, nourished'. These are dhatiyyat that constitute the essence.
Ghazali includes more than just genus and differentia; he lists multiple essential attributes that together spell out the quiddity.
4
Contrast with accidental attributes in rasm examples
Ghazali then gives rasm for human: 'animal that walks on two legs, with broad nails, laughing'. These are accidents (awarid) or proper properties (khawass) that are not part of the essence but still uniquely distinguish the thing. For wine: 'liquid that ferments in the jar and throws foam'. Compare these to the hadd examples.
Rasm is also convertible with the name, but it does not provide knowledge of the essence.
5
Summarize the convertibility condition
Both hadd and rasm satisfy convertibility: they can replace the name and the name can replace them. The difference is that hadd uses essential attributes (dhatiyyat) while rasm uses accidental properties (awarid/khawass). This distinction is crucial for evaluating the depth of conceptual knowledge.
Ghazali does not subdivide rasm further, but later logicians do.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول : في قوانين الحدود وفيه فصول
الفصل الأول : في بيان الحاجة إلى الحد، وقد قدمنا أن العلم قسمان : أحدهما : علم بذوات الأشياء ويُسمّى تصوّراً. والثاني : علم بنسبة تلك الذوات بعضها إلى بعض بسلب أو إيجاب ويُسمّى تصديقاً. وإنّ الوصول إلى التصديق بالحجّة والوصول إلى التصوّر التام بالحدّ . فإن الأشياء الموجودة تنقسم إلى أعيان شخصية كزيد ومكة وهذه الشجرة، وإلى أمور كلية كالإنسان والبلد والشجر والبرّ والخمر، وقد عرفت الفرق بين الكلّي والجزئي؛ وغرضنا في الكليات إذاً المستعملة في البراهين، والكلّي تارة يفهم فهماً جملياً كالمفهوم من مجرد اسم الجملة وسائر الأسماء والألقاب للأنواع والأجناس، وقد يفهم فهماً ملخصاً مفصلاً محيطاً بجميع الذاتيات التي بها قوام الشيء متميزاً عن غيره في الذهن تميزاً تاماً، ينعكس على الاسم وينعكس عليه الاسم كما يفهم من قولنا شراب مسكر مُعتصر من العنب، وحيوان ناطق ذو نفس حسّاس مُتحرّك بالإرادة مُتغذّ، فإن هذه الحدود يفهم بها الخمر والإنسان والحيوان فهماً أشدّ تلخيصاً وتفصيلاً وتحقيقاً وتمييزاً مما يفهم من مجرّد أساميها، وما يفهم الشيء هذا الضرب من التفهيم يُسمّى حدا، كما أن ما يفهم الضرب الأول من التفهيم يُسمّى اسماً ولقباً والفهم الحاصل من التحديد يُسمّى علماً ملخصاً مفصلاً، والعلم الحاصل بمجرد الاسم يُسمى علماً جملياً. وقد يفهم الشيء مما يتميّز به عن غيره بحيث ينعكس على اسمه وينعكس الاسم عليه ويتميّز لا بالصفات الذاتية المقومة التي هي الأجناس والأنواع والفصول، بل بالعوارض والخواص فَيُسمّى ذلك رسماً، كقولنا في تمييز الإنسان عن غيره : إنه الحيوان الماشي برجلين العريض الأظفار، الضحاك، فإن هذا يميّزه عن غيره كالحد وكقولك في الخمر: إنه المائع المستحيل في الدن الذي يقذف
The first art: on the laws of definitions, and it contains chapters. The first chapter: on explaining the need for definition. We have already stated that knowledge is two kinds: one of them is knowledge of the essences of things and is called conceptualization (tasawwur). The second is knowledge of the relation of those essences to each other by negation or affirmation and is called assent (tasdiq). And reaching assent is by proof, and reaching complete conceptualization is by definition. For existing things are divided into individual entities such as Zayd, Mecca, and this tree, and into universal matters such as human, city, tree, land, and wine. And you have known the difference between the universal and the particular. Our purpose then is in the universals used in proofs. The universal is sometimes understood summarily, like the understanding from the mere name of the term and other names and titles of species and genera, and it is sometimes understood in a detailed summarized way encompassing all the essential attributes (dhatiyyat) by which the thing is constituted, distinguished from others in the mind with complete distinction, such that it is convertible with the name and the name is convertible with it, as understood from our saying: an intoxicating drink pressed from grapes, and a rational animal possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will, nourished. For these definitions understand wine, human, and animal with a stronger summarization, detail, verification, and distinction than what is understood from their mere names. And what understands a thing by this kind of understanding is called a definition (hadd), just as what understands the first kind of understanding is called a name and title. And the understanding obtained from definition is called summarized detailed knowledge, and the knowledge obtained by the mere name is called summary knowledge. And a thing may be understood by what distinguishes it from others such that it is convertible with its name and the name is convertible with it, and it is distinguished not by the essential constitutive attributes which are genera, species, and differentiae, but by accidents and proper properties, so that is called a description (rasm), like our saying in distinguishing humans from others: it is the animal that walks on two legs, with broad nails, laughing. For this distinguishes it from others like the definition, and like your saying concerning wine: it is the liquid that ferments in the jar which throws [foam].
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition from the opening of Ghazali's Miyar al-Ilm, explaining the need for definitions (hudud) and the two types: genuine definition (hadd) and description (rasm). It begins by recapping the division of knowledge into tasawwur (conceptualization) and tasdiq (assent), linking tasawwur to definition and tasdiq to proof. Then it distinguishes between particulars (like 'Zayd') and universals (like 'human'), noting that the purpose of logic concerns universals used in demonstrations. The universal can be understood summarily (jumli) merely through its name, or in a detailed, summarized way that enumerates its essential attributes (dhatiyyat) that constitute its essence. This detailed understanding is called hadd, and it yields detailed conceptual knowledge. Ghazali provides examples: for wine ('an intoxicating drink pressed from grapes'), for human ('a rational animal possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will, nourished'). Then he introduces rasm: a thing may be understood not through essential constitutive attributes but through accidental properties and proper properties (khawass) that still uniquely distinguish it, like 'walking on two legs, broad nails, laughing' for human, or 'liquid that ferments in the jar and throws foam' for wine. This rasm functions like a definition but uses non-essential features. The key distinction is that hadd uses the essence (genus and differentia), while rasm uses accidents or properties that are exclusive to the thing. This passage is foundational for understanding the logical tools of definition and description.

Jumli vs Mufassal Understanding and the Definition of Hadd
Author contrasts jumli and mufassal understanding and defines hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between summary (jumli) and detailed (mufassal) understanding, and identify a complete hadd (definition) based on its essential attributes.

Core Idea
A definition (hadd) is not just a label; it reveals the essence by listing all essential attributes.

In this passage, the author explains that there are two levels of knowing a universal. The first is a mere name, giving a vague idea. The second is a full definition that specifies every essential attribute. This lesson will help you tell the difference and recognize what makes a true definition, using examples from wine and man.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of understanding
Find in the Arabic the phrases for 'summary understanding' (فهماً جملياً) and 'detailed summarized understanding' (فهماً ملخصاً مفصلاً). Notice that the first comes from a mere name, while the second comes from a definition.
2
Examine the examples of hadd
The author gives definitions of wine and man. List the essential attributes (dhatiyyat) mentioned in each definition. For wine: 'intoxicating drink expressed from grapes.' For man: 'rational animal possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will, nourished.'
3
Check the completeness condition
According to the author, a hadd must encompass all essential attributes and fully distinguish the thing. Verify if the example for man covers all that is essential for humanity (e.g., rationality, animality, life, will, nourishment).
4
Understand the bidirectional relationship
The author states that the definition applies to the name and the name applies to the definition, meaning they are coextensive. Why is this important? Because a complete definition must exactly match the referent of the name without remainder or excess.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلّي تارة يفهم فهماً جملياً كالمفهوم من مجرد اسم الجملة وسائر الأسماء والألقاب للأنواع والأجناس، وقد يفهم فهماً ملخصاً مفصلاً محيطاً بجميع الذاتيات التي بها قوام الشيء متميزاً عن غيره في الذهن تميزاً تاماً، ينعكس على الاسم وينعكس عليه الاسم كما يفهم من قولنا شراب مسكر مُعتصر من العنب، وحيوان ناطق ذو نفس حسّاس مُتحرّك بالإرادة مُتغذّ، فإن هذه الحدود يفهم بها الخمر والإنسان والحيوان فهماً أشدّ تلخيصاً وتفصيلاً وتحقيقاً وتمييزاً مما يفهم من مجرّد أساميها، وما يفهم الشيء هذا الضرب من التفهيم يُسمّى حدا، كما أن ما يفهم الضرب الأول من التفهيم يُسمّى اسماً ولقباً والفهم الحاصل من التحديد يُسمّى علماً ملخصاً مفصلاً، والعلم الحاصل بمجرد الاسم يُسمى علماً جملياً.
The universal is sometimes understood by a summary understanding, like what is understood from the mere name of the term and other names and titles for species and genera; and sometimes it is understood by a summarized, detailed understanding encompassing all the essential attributes (dhatiyyat) by which the thing is constituted, distinguished from others in the mind with a complete distinction, such that the definition applies to the name and the name applies to it, as is understood from our saying: 'an intoxicating drink expressed from grapes,' and 'a rational animal possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will, nourished.' For these definitions convey wine, man, and animal with a stronger summarization, detail, verification, and distinction than what is understood from their mere names. That which conveys a thing by this type of understanding is called a 'hadd' (definition), just as that which conveys the first type of understanding is called a name or title. The understanding obtained from definition is called a detailed, summarized science, and the understanding obtained from mere name is called a summary science.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage expands on the two levels of understanding a universal. The first level is jumli (summary), which is what one gets from merely knowing the name or title of a species or genus—e.g., knowing the word 'wine' without knowing its essence. The second level is mufassal (detailed), achieved through a hadd (definition). The author gives two examples: for wine, the definition 'an intoxicating drink expressed from grapes'; for man, 'a rational animal possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will, nourished.' These definitions list the essential attributes (dhatiyyat) that make up the thing and fully distinguish it from others. The author emphasizes that such definitions have a bidirectional relationship with the name: the definition applies to the name's referent and the name applies to the definition's referent (coextensive). The passage then formally defines the term 'hadd': it is what conveys a thing in this detailed manner, while the first type of understanding (from mere name) is called 'ism' (name) or 'laqab' (title). The knowledge from hadd is called 'detailed summarized science', and from mere name 'summary science'. This clarifies that definitions are not just labels but provide comprehensive essential knowledge.

فهماً ملخصاً مفصلاً

Key Takeaway

Hadd and Rasm

Rasm and Complete/Incomplete Hadd and Rasm
Author defines rasm and types of hadd and rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a complete and incomplete rasm and classify a given description as hadd or rasm.

Core Idea
Hadd uses essence; rasm uses accidents; both can be complete or incomplete.

In the previous lessons, you learned about hadd (definition) using essential attributes. This passage introduces rasm (description), which achieves distinction through accidental properties. The author explains when rasm is necessary and how both hadd and rasm divide into complete and incomplete types. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its description
Read the Arabic carefully. Find the term being described and the attributes given. For example, the author describes 'man' as 'the animal that walks on two legs, with wide nails, laughing'. Note whether these attributes are essential or accidental.
In Islamic contexts, consider descriptions like 'salah is a ritual prayer' – is that essential or accidental?
2
Distinguish essential from accidental attributes
Check if the attributes are constitutive of the thing's essence (e.g., genus and differentia) or merely accompanying properties. The author says hadd uses essential (dhatiyyat), rasm uses accidents (a'rad) and properties (khawass).
For example, 'walking on two legs' is an accident for man, while 'rational animal' is essential.
3
Assess completeness
A complete description (tamm) is coextensive with the name – it applies to all instances and only those. An incomplete one (naqis) is more general, omitting some restrictions. Check if the description would include any extra things or miss any actual members.
Complete: 'liquid that ferments in vat and throws up froth' is coextensive with wine? The author says if all such accidents are collected, they are found only in wine.
4
Classify as hadd or rasm, tamm or naqis
Apply the author's formal definitions: hadd indicates quiddity (mahiyya) using essential attributes; rasm is composed of accidents/properties peculiar to the thing and coextensive with it. Then determine if it's complete (coextensive) or incomplete (more general).
For example, 'man is a rational animal' is hadd tamm; 'man is a laughing animal' is rasm tamm if laughing is unique to man.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يفهم الشيء مما يتميّز به عن غيره بحيث ينعكس على اسمه وينعكس الاسم عليه ويتميّز لا بالصفات الذاتية المقومة التي هي الأجناس والأنواع والفصول، بل بالعوارض والخواص فَيُسمّى ذلك رسماً، كقولنا في تمييز الإنسان عن غيره : إنه الحيوان الماشي برجلين العريض الأظفار، الضحاك، فإن هذا يميّزه عن غيره كالحد وكقولك في الخمر: إنه المائع المستحيل في الدن الذي يقذف بالزبد إلى غير ذلك من العوارض التي إذا جمعت لم توجد إلا للخمر، وهذا إذا كان أعم من الشيء المحدود بأن يترك بعض الاحترازات سُمِّي رسماً ناقصاً، كما أن الحد إذا ترك فيه بعض الفصول الذاتية فيكون سمّي حداً ناقصاً، وربّ شيء يعسر الوقوف على جميع ذاتياته أولاً يلفى لها عبارة، فيعدل إلى الاحترازات العرضية بدلاً عن الفصول الذاتية فيكون رسماً مميزاً قائماً مقام الحد في التمييز فقط لا في تفهيم جميع الذاتيات والملخصون إنما يطلبون من الحد تصوّر كنه الشيء وتمثل حقيقته في نفوسهم، لا لمجرّد التمييز ولكن مهما حصل التصوّر بكماله تبعه التمييز ومن يطلب التمييز المجرّد يقتنع بالرسم فقد عرفت ما ينتهي إليه تأثير الاسم والحدّ والرسم في تفهيم الأشياء، وعرفت انقسام تصوّر الأشياء إلى تصوّر له بمعرفة ذاتياته المفصلة وإلى تصوّر له بمعرفة أعراضه وأن كلّ واحدٍ منهما قد يكون تاماً مساوياً للاسم في طرفي الحمل، وقد يكون ناقصاً فيكون أعمّ من الاسم. واعلم أن أنفع الرسوم في تعريف الأشياء أن يوضع فيه الجنس القريب أصلاً ثم تذكر الأعراض الخاصة المشهورة فصولاً، فإن الخاصة الخفية إذا ذكرت لم تفد التعريف على العموم، فمهما قلت في رسم المثلث أنه الشكل الذي زواياه تساوي قائمتين لم تكن رسمته إلا للمهندس، فإذن الحدّ قول دال على ماهية الشيء، والرسم هو القول المؤلّف من أعراض الشيء وخواصه التي تخصه جملتها بالاجتماع وتُساويه .
And sometimes a thing is understood by what distinguishes it from others such that the distinguishing description applies to the name and the name applies to it, and it is distinguished not by the constitutive essential attributes which are genera, species, and differentiae, but by accidents and properties; that is called a 'rasm' (description). For example, in distinguishing man from others: he is the animal that walks on two legs, with wide nails, laughing. For this distinguishes him like a definition. And like your saying in wine: it is the liquid that ferments in the vat and throws up froth, and other accidents which when collected are found only in wine. And if this [rasm] is more general than the defined thing, by leaving out some restrictions, it is called an incomplete rasm. Similarly, if in a definition some essential differentiae are left out, it is called an incomplete definition. Sometimes it is difficult to ascertain all the essential attributes of a thing, or there is no expression for them, so one resorts to accidental restrictions instead of essential differentiae, and that becomes a distinguishing rasm standing in place of the definition in distinction only, not in conveying all the essential attributes. The investigators seek from definition the conception of the quiddity of the thing and the representation of its reality in their souls, not merely for distinction; but whenever conception is complete, distinction follows it. And whoever seeks mere distinction is content with rasm. So you now know the reach of the effect of name, definition, and rasm in conveying things. And you know the division of conception of things into conception by knowledge of its detailed essential attributes and conception by knowledge of its accidents, and that each of these may be complete, coextensive with the name in both terms of predication, or incomplete, being more general than the name. And know that the most useful of descriptions in defining things is that in which the proximate genus is placed as a root, then the specific and well-known accidents are mentioned as differentiae. For if obscure properties are mentioned, they do not help in definition for the general public. Whenever you say in describing a triangle that it is a figure whose angles equal two right angles, you have only described it for a geometer. Therefore, a definition is a statement indicating the quiddity of a thing, and a description is a statement composed of the accidents and properties of a thing which, in their totality, are peculiar to it and coextensive with it.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of rasm (description) as an alternative to hadd (definition). While hadd uses essential attributes (dhatiyyat: genera, species, differentiae), rasm uses accidental attributes (a'rad) and properties (khawass) to distinguish a thing. The key is that rasm also must be coextensive with the name (in'ikas). The author gives examples: for man, 'the animal that walks on two legs, with wide nails, laughing'; for wine, 'the liquid that ferments in the vat and throws up froth' plus other accidents that collectively are unique to wine. The passage then explains that both hadd and rasm can be complete (tamm) or incomplete (naqis). A complete hadd includes all essential differentiae; an incomplete hadd omits some. Similarly, a complete rasm includes all necessary accidents to be coextensive; an incomplete rasm is more general (a'am) because it lacks some restrictions. The author notes that sometimes it is impossible to list all essential attributes, so one resorts to accidental properties as a substitute for essential differentiae, yielding a distinguishing rasm that only serves for distinction, not for conveying the full essence. The 'investigators' (mulakhkhasun) seek from definition the conception of the quiddity (kunh) itself, not mere distinction, but distinction follows automatically. The passage concludes with a practical recommendation: the most useful rasm places the proximate genus first, then lists well-known specific accidents as differentiae; obscure properties are not helpful for general audiences. Finally, two formal definitions are given: 'hadd is a statement indicating the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing' and 'rasm is a statement composed of a thing's accidents and properties that collectively are peculiar to it and coextensive with it'. This settles the technical terminology.
