Constructing a Proper Definition: Matter and Form
Author explains the matter and form of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a correct definition by identifying the proximate genus and listing all essential differentiae in order for any given term in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A correct definition uses the nearest genus and all essential differentiae, nothing more, nothing less.

In this passage, the author explains that every definition has matter (the genera, species, and differentiae) and form (their correct arrangement). The key rule is to always use the proximate genus — the nearest category above the species — and then list all essential differentiae in sequence. This ensures the definition captures the essence without unnecessary extras. In this lesson, you will learn to apply these rules to terms from Islamic disciplines like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined (al-mahdud).
Start by naming the thing you want to define. For example, in the passage the author defines 'man' (al-insan) and 'wine' (al-khamr). This focuses the definition.
2
Find the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarib).
Determine the closest category that includes the term. For man, it is 'animal' (hayawan) — not 'body', because 'animal' is intermediate between body and man. For wine, it is 'drink' (sharab), not 'liquid'.
Avoid using a remote genus; the proximate genus gives more specific information.
3
List all essential differentiae (al-fusul al-dhatiyya) in order.
Identify the attributes that distinguish the species within the genus. For man, the essential differentia is 'rational' (natiq). The passage implies that 'mortal' should not be included because it is not essential. For wine, the essential differentia is 'intoxicating' (muskir). All essential differentiae must be included and arranged in the correct sequence.
Essential differentiae are those without which the species cannot be conceived.
4
Verify that the definition is complete and not circular.
Check that the definition includes all instances of the definiendum and excludes everything else. For example, 'rational animal' includes all humans and excludes all non-humans. Also ensure that the definition is not circular (the definiendum should not appear in the definiens).
The passage emphasizes leaving nothing out and not using the proximate genus incorrectly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : في مادة الحد وصورته قد قدمنا أن كلّ مؤلّف فله مادّة وصورة كما في القياس، ومادة الحدّ الأجناس والأنواع والفصول وقد ذكرناها في كتاب مقدّمات القياس . وأما صورته وهيئته فهو أن يُراعى فيه إيراد الجنس الأقرب ويردف بالفصول الذاتية كلّها، فلا يترك منها شيء، ونعني بإيراد الجنس القريب أن لا نقول في حد الإنسان : جسم ناطق مائت، وإن كان ذلك مساوياً للمطلوب بل نقول : حيوان، فإن الحيوان متوسط بين الجسم والإنسان فهو أقرب إلى المطلوب من الجسم، ولا نقول في حد الخمر: إنه مائع مُسكر بل نقول : شراب مُسکر؛ فإنه أخص من المائع وأقرب منه إلى الخمر، وكذلك ينبغي أن يورد جميع الفصول الذاتية على الترتيب.
Chapter Two: On the matter and form of definition. We have already stated that every composite has matter and form, as in the syllogism. The matter of definition is the genera, species, and differentiae, which we have mentioned in the book of Premises of Syllogism. As for its form and structure, it is that one observes bringing the proximate genus and then follows it with all the essential differentiae, leaving nothing out. We mean by bringing the proximate genus that we do not say in the definition of man: 'a rational, mortal body', although that is coextensive with the intended, but rather we say: 'an animal' because animal is intermediate between body and man, thus it is closer to the intended than body. And we do not say in the definition of wine: 'an intoxicating liquid', but rather we say: 'an intoxicating drink' because it is more specific than liquid and closer to wine. Likewise all the essential differentiae should be listed in order.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This chapter addresses the material and formal components of a definition. The author first notes that every composite has matter and form, analogous to the syllogism. The matter of a definition consists of genera, species, and differentiae, which were covered in a previous work (the Premises of Syllogism, likely the first part of the book). The form, or structure, requires two things: (1) that the proximate genus (al-jins al-aqrab) be mentioned, and (2) that all essential differentiae be listed in order, without omission. The author illustrates the proximate genus rule with two examples. For man, one should not say 'a rational, mortal body' because body is a remote genus; the proximate genus is 'animal' (hayawan), which is a middle term between body and man. Similarly, for wine, one should not say 'an intoxicating liquid' because liquid is a remote genus; the proximate genus is 'drink' (sharab), which is more specific and closer to wine. This ensures the definition is as informative as possible, starting from the nearest genus. The passage concludes that all essential differentiae must be mentioned in order, implying a sequence that builds up the definition, e.g., for man: animal + rational, perhaps also sensitive, etc. This completes the basic rules for constructing a proper definition.

Complete and Incomplete Definitions and Descriptions
Author distinguishes complete/incomplete definitions and descriptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given definition or description as complete or incomplete by checking its genus and differentiae or accidents.

Core Idea
Complete definitions require all essential differentiae; descriptions use accidents when essence is unknown.

In Islamic logic, definitions (حدود) and descriptions (رسوم) are tools for conceptualization. But not all are equal: some are complete, some incomplete. This lesson teaches you to evaluate them so you can use definitions critically when studying fiqh, kalam, or other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify type: definition or description
Look for key terms: if it includes essential genus (جنس قريب) and differentiae (فصول ذاتية), it's a definition (حد). If it uses accidents (أعراض) or properties (خواص) without essential differentiae, it's a description (رسم). The passage ends with formal definitions of each.
The author emphasizes that definition aims at quiddity, description at mere distinction.
2
Check completeness of definition
A complete definition (حد تام) must include the nearest genus and all essential differentiae. If any essential differentia is omitted, it is an incomplete definition (حد ناقص). Example: 'Human is a rational animal' is incomplete if it omits 'mortal'.
The passage states: 'إذا ترك فيه بعض الفصول الذاتية فيكون سمّي حداً ناقصاً'.
3
Check completeness of description
A complete description (رسم تام) uses accidents that together are peculiar to the thing and equivalent to it. If it is more general (leaves out restrictions), it is incomplete (رسم ناقص). Example: describing wine as 'what has froth and foam' without further restrictions is incomplete.
The passage says: 'إذا كان أعم من الشيء المحدود بأن يترك بعض الاحترازات سُمِّي رسماً ناقصاً'.
4
Identify the purpose and choose appropriately
The thorough seeker wants a complete definition to grasp quiddity; one who only needs distinction can settle for a description. The author advises using the nearest genus with well-known accidents for the most beneficial description.
Hidden properties only help specialists, not the general audience.

with froth and other accidents which, when collected, are found only in wine. This, if it is more general than the defined thing by leaving out some restrictions, is called an incomplete description (رسم ناقص), just as the definition, if some essential differentiae are omitted from it, is called an incomplete definition (حد ناقص). Sometimes it is difficult to ascertain all the essentials of something, or no expression is found for them, so one resorts to accidental restrictions instead of essential differentiae, thus making a distinctive description (رسم) that takes the place of definition in distinction only, not in informing of all the essentials. The thorough ones seek from definition the conceptualization of the quiddity of the thing and the representation of its reality in their minds, not merely for distinction; but whenever conceptualization is attained completely, distinction follows from it. Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes complete and incomplete definitions (حد) and descriptions (رسم) in Islamic logic. The author begins by illustrating an incomplete description with the example of wine: if one lists accidents like froth that are unique to wine when combined, but leaves out some restricting features, the description becomes more general than the defined thing and thus incomplete. Similarly, a definition lacking some essential differentiae is incomplete. The passage then explains that since it may be impossible to capture all essentials or find terms for them, one may resort to accidental properties to create a distinctive description that serves only for distinguishing the thing, not for conveying its full essence. The author emphasizes that the true purpose of definition is to achieve a conceptualization (تصور) of the thing's quiddity (كنه) and represent its reality in the mind; distinction follows automatically. Mere distinction seekers are satisfied with description. The passage then classifies conceptualization into two types: one through essential detailed knowledge, and one through accidents. Each can be complete and coextensive with the name or incomplete and broader. The author advises that the most useful description uses the nearest genus as basis and adds well-known specific accidents as differentiae; hidden properties help only specialists, as in the definition of a triangle by its angle sum, which only an engineer understands. Finally, concise formal definitions are given: definition is a statement indicating the quiddity of a thing; description is a statement composed of accidents and properties that in their entirety are peculiar to and equivalent to the thing.

Conditions of a Complete Definition (Hadd Tāmm)
Author details conditions for complete definition and errors thereof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition is complete or incomplete, and evaluate the effect of adding accidental attributes or omitting differentiae.

Core Idea
A complete definition requires all essential attributes in order; omissions harm, but added accidents do not invalidate.

This passage from Ghazali's Mi'yār al-'Ilm details the standards for a perfect definition. After introducing an example of defining 'animal', he explains that a complete definition (hadd tāmm) must include all essential differentiae along with the proximate genus. He then addresses common errors—deficiency, excess, and substitution—and assesses their impact on the definition's purpose: conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) of the thing's essence. A key insight is that adding accidental attributes does not ruin the definition, while replacing essentials with accidents does. This nuanced position helps us avoid rigid formalism when evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential components of a definition
A complete definition consists of the proximate genus (jins qarīb) and all essential differentiae (fuṣūl dhātiyya). For example, 'animal' is defined as 'a body possessing a sentient soul, having extension, moving by will'. The genus 'body' and the differentiae 'sentient soul', 'extension', 'moving by will' are essential.
The passage stresses that mentioning the genus implies its differentiae implicitly, but a complete definition makes them explicit.
2
Recognize deficiency (naqṣ) in definition
If one omits any essential differentia, the definition becomes incomplete (hadd nāqiṣ). Even if distinction is still achieved, the conceptualization of the thing's full essence is lacking. Example: defining 'animal' without 'moving by will' still distinguishes animals from plants, but misses the complete essence.
Deficiency harms the purpose of detailed conceptual knowledge.
3
Recognize excess (ziyāda) and substitution (ibdāl)
Excess can be of two kinds: adding accidental attributes (ʻaraḍiyyāt) or replacing the genus with its own definition (e.g., using 'body possessing a sentient soul...' instead of 'animal'). The author says adding accidents does not impair conceptualization and may help clarification, but replacing essential attributes with accidents harms perfection. Also, lengthening by defining the genus instead of naming it is superfluous.
The purpose of definition is to include all essential attributes either in potency (by mentioning the genus) or in act (by listing differentiae).
4
Apply the criteria to an Islamic science example
Take the definition of 'ṣalāh' (ritual prayer). A complete definition would include its genus (ibādah, worship) and differentiae (specific actions, conditions, and intentions). Omitting the requirement of facing the qibla might yield an incomplete definition. Adding an accidental attribute like 'preferably at the mosque' would not invalidate it. Replacing essential conditions with mere recommendations would impair the definition's accuracy.
Jurists use such criteria to ensure legal definitions capture the essence of acts and objects of worship.

وإن كان التمييز يحصل ببعض الفصول. وإذا سُئل عن حدّ الحيوان فقال : جسم ذو نفس حسّاس له بعد متحرّك بالإرادة، فقد أتى بجميع الفصول ولو ترك ما بعد الحسّاس لكان التمييز حاصلاً به ولكن لا يكون قد تصوّر الحيوان بكمال ذاتياته . والحدّ عنوان المحدود فينبغي أن يكون مساوياً له في المعنى، فإن نقص بعض هذه الفصول سمّي حدا ناقصاً وإن كان التمييز حاصلاً به وكان مطرداً منعكساً في طريق الحمل، ومهما ذكر الجنس القريب وأتى بجميع الفصول الذاتية فلا ينبغي أن يزيد عليه . ومهما عرفت هذه الشروط في صورة الحدّ ومادته عرفت أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون له إلا حدّ واحد وأنه لا يحتمل الإيجاز والتطويل لأن إيجازه بحذف بعض الفصول وهو نقصان، وتطويله بذكر حدّ الجنس القريب بدل الجنس كقولك في حد الإنسان : إنه جسم ذو نفس حسّاس متحرّك بالإرادة ناطق مائت، فذكر حد الحيوان بدل الحيوان وهو فضول يُستغنى عنه، فإن المقصود أن يشتمل الحدّ على جميع ذاتيات الشيء إما بالقوة وإما بالفعل. ومهما ذكر الحيوان فقد اشتمل على الحسّاس والمتحرّك والجسم بالقوة أي على طريق التضمن، وكذلك قد للشيء الذي هو مركب من صورة ومادة بذكر أحدهما ، كما يقال في حد الغضب : القلب، وهذا ذكر المادة، ويقال : إنه طلب الانتقام، وهذا هو الصورة، بل الحدّ التام أن يقال : هو غليان دم القلب لطلب الانتقام . فإن قيل : فلو سها ساءٍ أو تعمّد مُتعمّد فَطَوَّل الحد بذكر حدّ الجنس القريب بدل الجنس القريب أو زاد على بعض الفصول الذاتية شيئاً من الأعراض واللوازم، أو نقص بعض الفصول فهل يفوت مقصود الحدّ كما يفوت مقصود القياس بالخطأ في صورته ؟ قلنا : الناظرون إلى ظواهر الأمور ربما يستعظمون الأمر في مثل هذا الخطأ، والأمر أهون مما يظنون مهما لاحظ الإنسان مقصود الحد، لأن المقصود تصوّر الشيء بجميع مقوماته مع مراعاة الترتيب بمعرفة الأعم والأخص بإيراد الأعم أولاً وإردافه بالأخص الجاري مجرى الفصول، وإذا حفظ ذلك فقد حصل العلم التصوري المفصل المطلوب. أما النقصان بترك بعض الفصول فإنه نقصان في التصوّر. وأما زيادة بعض الأعراض فلا يقدح فيما حصل من التصوّر الكامل، وقد ينتفع به في بعض المواضع في زيادة الكشف والإيضاح وأما إبدال الذاتيات باللوازم والعرضيات فذلك قادح في كمال التصوّر، فليعلم مبلغ تأثير كلّ واحد في المقصود ، ولا ينبغي أن يجمد الإنسان على الرسم المعتاد المألوف في كلّ أمره وينسى غرضه المطلوب، فإذن مهما عرف جميع الذاتيات على الترتيب حصل المقصود، وإن زيد شيء من الأعراض أو أخذ حدّ الجنس القريب بدل الجنس .
And if distinction is obtained by some of the differentiae. If one is asked about the definition of animal and says: 'a body possessing a sentient soul, having extension, moving by will', then he has brought all the differentiae, even if he left out what is after 'sentient', distinction would still be obtained by it, but he would not have conceptualized animal with the completeness of its essential attributes. And the definition is the title of the definiendum, so it should be equal to it in meaning. If some of these differentiae are omitted, it is called an incomplete definition, even if distinction is obtained by it and it is coextensive and converse in the mode of predication. And whenever the proximate genus is mentioned and all the essential differentiae are brought, one should not add to it. And whenever you know these conditions in the form and matter of the definition, you know that a single thing has only one definition and that it does not admit of shortening or lengthening, because shortening is by dropping some differentiae, which is deficiency, and lengthening is by mentioning the definition of the proximate genus instead of the genus, as when you say in the definition of man: 'it is a body possessing a sentient soul, moving by will, rational, mortal', so you mentioned the definition of animal instead of animal, which is superfluous and dispensable. For the purpose is that the definition includes all the essential attributes of the thing, either in potency or in act. And whenever animal is mentioned, it includes sentient, moving, and body in potency, i.e., by way of implication. Similarly, it is possible for a thing composed of form and matter to be defined by mentioning one of the two, as is said in the definition of anger: 'the heart'—this is mentioning the matter, and it is said: 'it is the desire for revenge'—this is the form; rather the complete definition is to say: 'it is the boiling of the blood of the heart for the desire for revenge.' If it is said: 'If someone errs or intentionally lengthens the definition by mentioning the definition of the proximate genus instead of the proximate genus, or adds to some essential differentiae something from accidents and concomitants, or omits some differentiae, does the purpose of definition become lost as the purpose of syllogism is lost by error in its form?' We say: Those who look at outward appearances might consider such an error grave, but the matter is easier than they think when one observes the purpose of definition, because the purpose is conceptualizing the thing with all its constituents while observing the order by knowing the more general and more specific, by bringing the more general first and following it with the more specific that follows the course of differentiae. And if that is preserved, then the desired detailed conceptual knowledge is obtained. As for deficiency by leaving some differentiae, it is a deficiency in conceptualization. As for adding some accidents, it does not impair the complete conceptualization obtained, and it may be beneficial in some places for increasing disclosure and clarification. And as for replacing essential attributes with concomitants and accidents, that impairs the perfection of conceptualization. So let one know the extent of each one's effect on the purpose, and one should not rigidly adhere to the usual customary form in all his affairs and forget his desired goal. So then, whenever all the essential attributes are known in order, the purpose is obtained, even if something of accidents is added or the definition of the proximate genus is taken instead of the genus.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the conditions for a complete definition (hadd tāmm) and the effects of various errors in definition. The author, Ghazali, begins by noting that a definition may still yield distinction even if some differentiae are omitted, but it would not capture the full essence. He stresses that the definition must be equal in meaning to the definiendum. Omitting differentiae yields an incomplete definition (hadd nāqiṣ). When the proximate genus and all essential differentiae are mentioned, nothing should be added. From these conditions, one infers that a single thing has only one definition, and it cannot be shortened or lengthened improperly. Shortening by dropping differentiae is a deficiency; lengthening by replacing the genus with its own definition (e.g., defining man by 'body...rational, mortal' instead of 'animal, rational') is superfluous. The purpose is to include all essential attributes either potentially or actually; mentioning the genus implies its differentiae potentially. For a composite of form and matter, one may define by one part, but the complete definition includes both, as in 'anger is the boiling of the blood of the heart for revenge'. Then a question arises: if someone makes errors—lengthening, adding accidents, omitting differentiae—does the definition fail like a syllogism fails with formal error? The author responds that such errors are not as grave as they seem, because the goal is conceptual knowledge of the thing's constituents in order. Omission causes deficiency in conceptualization. Adding accidents does not impair the complete conceptualization and may even help. But replacing essential attributes with accidents harms perfection. The author concludes that one should not be rigidly attached to customary forms but focus on the goal. If all essential attributes are known in order, the purpose is achieved even with added accidents or substituting the genus's definition. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that any deviation from a standard definition renders it useless.

Why Substituting Accidents for Essence Damages Definitions
Author warns against substituting essential with accidental in definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition replaces essential properties with accidental ones and explain why that harms the completeness of the conception.

Core Idea
A definition must capture the essence; swapping in accidents corrupts the mental representation.

In this passage, the author warns against a common pitfall: replacing the essential properties (al-dhātiyyāt) of a thing with its necessary concomitants (al-lawāzim) or accidental properties (al-ʿaraḍiyyāt). Such substitution damages the completeness of our conception (al-taṣawwur), because a definition's purpose is to present the quiddity. The author also cautions against blindly following conventional descriptions without regard for the intended goal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three property categories
The passage mentions three types: essential properties (al-dhātiyyāt), necessary concomitants (al-lawāzim), and accidents (al-ʿaraḍiyyāt). Understand the distinction: essentials constitute the quiddity, while accidents and concomitants are external to it.
Essentials are necessary for the thing to be what it is; accidents may or may not be present.
2
Understand the harm of substitution
The author states that replacing essentials with non-essentials is 'detrimental to the completeness of the conception.' This means the mental image of the thing becomes incomplete or distorted because the definition no longer captures the essence.
3
Recognize the permissible exceptions
The passage notes that if all essential properties are known in order, adding an accident or using the proximate genus instead of the remote genus does not harm the definition. The key condition is that the essential order is preserved.
This shows that accidents can be included as long as they do not replace essentials.
4
Apply to a sample definition
Given a definition from an Islamic science, check whether it lists only essential properties or includes accidents as substitutes. For example, a definition of 'ṣalāh' that includes conditions like facing the qibla (which is a condition, not part of essence) would be acceptable only if the essential actions (rukūʿ, sujūd, etc.) are already present.
Practice identifying what is essential vs. accidental in real definitions.

Source Text

والإيضاح وأما إبدال الذاتيات باللوازم والعرضيات فذلك قادح في كمال التصوّر، فليعلم مبلغ تأثير كلّ واحد في المقصود ، ولا ينبغي أن يجمد الإنسان على الرسم المعتاد المألوف في كلّ أمره وينسى غرضه المطلوب، فإذن مهما عرف جميع الذاتيات على الترتيب حصل المقصود، وإن زيد شيء من الأعراض أو أخذ حدّ الجنس القريب بدل الجنس .
And clarification: As for replacing essential properties with necessary concomitants and accidents, that is detrimental to the completeness of the conception. So let one know the extent of the effect of each in the intended purpose. And one should not stagnate on the customary, familiar description in all of his affairs and forget his sought-after goal. For then, whenever all the essential properties are known in order, the purpose is achieved, even if some accident is added or the definition of the proximate genus is taken instead of the genus.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a warning about a common mistake in definition. The author emphasizes that a complete conception (tasawwur) of a thing requires knowing all its essential properties (dhatiyyat) in their proper order. Replacing these with accidents (arad) or necessary concomitants (lawazim) damages the completeness of the conception. Moreover, one should not mechanically follow a conventional description without considering the purpose of the definition. The goal is to achieve a mental representation that matches all essential attributes. Adding accidental properties or substituting a lower genus (genus proximum) for the true genus is permissible only if the essential order is preserved. The passage insists on the priority of essence over accident in definition.

The Prerequisite of Existence for Real Definition
Author explains that asking 'what is it?' presumes existence, and details the stepwise questioning from genus to differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition and a nominal definition, and identify when a 'what is it?' question actually seeks a nominal definition because existence is not established.

Core Idea
Real definition requires existence; without it, 'what is it?' only explains the name.

This passage from al-Miyar introduces a crucial rule for the seeker of definitions: you cannot ask 'what is it?' about something until you have established that it exists. Otherwise your question is not about the real essence but only about the meaning of the name. This distinction is vital for rigorous inquiry in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the order of inquiry
The author states that the questioner asks 'What is it?' only after finishing with 'Is it?'. Identify that 'Is it?' (هل) establishes existence.
2
Distinguish real from nominal definition
When existence is not believed, the question 'What is it?' becomes a request for sharḥ al-ism (explanation of the name). Find the example of al-khala' and alchemy.
sharḥ al-ism does not require the thing to exist.
3
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
Consider asking 'What is jinn?' Existence is established by revelation, so it can be a real definition. But asking 'What is a philosopher's stone?' without belief in its existence would be nominal.
4
Test your understanding
Given a scenario, decide whether a question is asking for real or nominal definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث : في ترتيب طلب الحدّ · والسائل عن الشيء بقوله : ما هو ؟ لا يسأل إلَّا بعد الفراغ عن مطلب «هل»، كما أن السائل بِلمَ لا يسأل إلا بعد الفراغ عن مطلب «هل»، فإن سأل عن الشيء قبل اعتقاد وجوده وقال : ما هو؟ رجع إلى طلب شرح الاسم، كقول القائل : ما الخلا وما الكيميا؟ وهو لا يعتقد لهما وجوداً، فإذا اعتقد الوجود كان الطلب متوجهاً إلى تصوّر الشيء في ذاته وترتيبه أن يقول : ما هو؟ مشيراً إلى نخلة مثلاً، فإذا أجاب المسؤول بالجنس القريب وقال شجرة، لم يقنع السائل به بل قرن بما ذكره صيغة أي وقال : أي شجرة هي ؟ فإذا قال : هي شجرة تثمر الرطب، فقد بلغ المقصود وانقطع السؤال إلَّا إذا لم يفهم معنى الرطب أو الشجر، فيعدل إلى صيغة ما ويقول : ما الرطب وما الشجر؟ فيذكر له جنسه وفصله فيقول : الشجر نبات قائم على ساق، فإن قال : ما الساق ؟ فيذكر جنسه وفصله ويقول: هو جسم مُغتذ نام، فإن قال : ما الجسم ؟ فيقول : هو الممتد في الأقطار الثلاثة أي هو الطويل العريض العميق، وهكذا إلى أن ينقطع السؤال .
Chapter Three: On the Order of Seeking the Definition. The questioner asking 'What is it?' does not ask except after finishing with the inquiry of 'Is it?', just as the questioner asking 'Why?' does not ask except after finishing with the inquiry of 'Is it?'. For if he asks about something before believing in its existence and says 'What is it?', it reverts to seeking the explanation of the name, like one who says 'What is al-khala' and what is alchemy?' while not believing they exist. So when he believes existence, the request becomes directed to the conception of the thing in its essence, and his manner is to say 'What is it?' pointing to a palm tree for example. So if the respondent answers with the proximate genus and says 'a tree', the questioner is not satisfied with that but joins to what he mentioned the form 'which' and says 'Which tree is it?' So if he says 'It is a tree that produces dates', then the purpose is reached and the questioning ends, unless he does not understand the meaning of 'dates' or 'tree', so he turns to the form 'what' and says 'What are dates?' and 'What is tree?' So he mentions its genus and differentia and says 'Tree is a plant standing on a trunk.' So if he says 'What is trunk?', he mentions its genus and differentia and says 'It is a nourished, growing body.' So if he says 'What is body?', he says 'It is that which extends in three dimensions, i.e., it is long, wide, and deep.' And so on until the questioning ends.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage outlines the proper procedure for seeking a definition. The author first establishes a fundamental rule: the question 'What is it?' (mā huwa) presupposes that the existence of the thing is already known. This is analogous to asking 'Why?' (lima) which also requires prior existence. If someone asks 'What is it?' without believing in existence, the question is actually about the explanation of the name (sharḥ al-ism), like asking 'What is khala'?' (a mythical creature) without believing it exists. Once existence is known, the inquirer wants a real definition (conception of the essence). The author then illustrates the process with the example of a palm tree. The answer begins with the proximate genus ('tree'), but the questioner is not satisfied and asks 'Which tree?' to get the differentia (the fruit-bearing quality). Once the differentia is given, the questioning ends unless a term in the answer is not understood. In that case, one asks about that term, and the process is repeated with its genus and differentia, moving to higher genera (plant, growing body, body extending in three dimensions) until the questioning naturally stops when the meanings are clear. This demonstrates that definition is a stepwise process from the most specific to the most general, ensuring complete understanding.

Key Terms
شرح الاسم
الجنس القريب
فصل

C) The thing must have a genus and differentia.
D) The questioner must already know the differentia.

Key Takeaway
The question 'What is it?' is only about real essence after existence is established; otherwise it is merely a nominal definition.

The Stepwise Method for Constructing Definitions: From Genus to Differentia
Author explains that asking 'what is it?' presumes existence, and details the stepwise questioning from genus to differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a correct definition by following the method of first providing the proximate genus, then the differentia, and recursively defining any unclear terms using the same procedure.

Core Idea
Definition is a staircase: start at the nearest genus, add the distinguishing differentia, and climb higher if a step is unclear.

The author illustrates the process of defining a palm tree as a model for all definitions. The answerer first gives the proximate genus ('tree'), then when asked 'which tree?', supplies the differentia ('produces dates'). If any term in the answer is not understood, it becomes a new definiendum, and the process repeats until primitive terms are reached. This method ensures clarity and completeness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term to define
Pick a concrete thing (e.g., palm tree in the passage).
2
Supply the proximate genus
The author says the respondent answers with 'al-jins al-qarib', here 'tree'. This is the immediate category.
Genus must be the nearest, not a remote one.
3
Ask for the differentia using 'any (ayy)'
The questioner is not satisfied with genus alone and uses the form 'which' (أي) to request the differentia. Realize that genus alone is insufficient.
4
Provide the differentia
The answer gives the distinguishing feature: 'produces dates'. Now the definition is complete.
Differentia must be specific to the species.
5
Recursively define any unclear term
If 'dates' or 'tree' is not understood, treat it as a new definiendum and repeat the process. The author does this for 'tree' (plant standing on trunk), then 'trunk' (nourished growing body), then 'body' (extended in three dimensions) until questioning stops.
This recursion continues until terms are self-evident or accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث : في ترتيب طلب الحدّ · والسائل عن الشيء بقوله : ما هو ؟ لا يسأل إلَّا بعد الفراغ عن مطلب «هل»، كما أن السائل بِلمَ لا يسأل إلا بعد الفراغ عن مطلب «هل»، فإن سأل عن الشيء قبل اعتقاد وجوده وقال : ما هو؟ رجع إلى طلب شرح الاسم، كقول القائل : ما الخلا وما الكيميا؟ وهو لا يعتقد لهما وجوداً، فإذا اعتقد الوجود كان الطلب متوجهاً إلى تصوّر الشيء في ذاته وترتيبه أن يقول : ما هو؟ مشيراً إلى نخلة مثلاً، فإذا أجاب المسؤول بالجنس القريب وقال شجرة، لم يقنع السائل به بل قرن بما ذكره صيغة أي وقال : أي شجرة هي ؟ فإذا قال : هي شجرة تثمر الرطب، فقد بلغ المقصود وانقطع السؤال إلَّا إذا لم يفهم معنى الرطب أو الشجر، فيعدل إلى صيغة ما ويقول : ما الرطب وما الشجر؟ فيذكر له جنسه وفصله فيقول : الشجر نبات قائم على ساق، فإن قال : ما الساق ؟ فيذكر جنسه وفصله ويقول: هو جسم مُغتذ نام، فإن قال : ما الجسم ؟ فيقول : هو الممتد في الأقطار الثلاثة أي هو الطويل العريض العميق، وهكذا إلى أن ينقطع السؤال .
Chapter Three: On the Order of Seeking the Definition. The questioner asking 'What is it?' does not ask except after finishing with the inquiry of 'Is it?', just as the questioner asking 'Why?' does not ask except after finishing with the inquiry of 'Is it?'. For if he asks about something before believing in its existence and says 'What is it?', it reverts to seeking the explanation of the name, like one who says 'What is al-khala' and what is alchemy?' while not believing they exist. So when he believes existence, the request becomes directed to the conception of the thing in its essence, and his manner is to say 'What is it?' pointing to a palm tree for example. So if the respondent answers with the proximate genus and says 'a tree', the questioner is not satisfied with that but joins to what he mentioned the form 'which' and says 'Which tree is it?' So if he says 'It is a tree that produces dates', then the purpose is reached and the questioning ends, unless he does not understand the meaning of 'dates' or 'tree', so he turns to the form 'what' and says 'What are dates?' and 'What is tree?' So he mentions its genus and differentia and says 'Tree is a plant standing on a trunk.' So if he says 'What is trunk?', he mentions its genus and differentia and says 'It is a nourished, growing body.' So if he says 'What is body?', he says 'It is that which extends in three dimensions, i.e., it is long, wide, and deep.' And so on until the questioning ends.

Commentary
This passage outlines the proper procedure for seeking a definition. The author first establishes a fundamental rule: the question 'What is it?' (mā huwa) presupposes that the existence of the thing is already known. This is analogous to asking 'Why?' (lima) which also requires prior existence. If someone asks 'What is it?' without believing in existence, the question is actually about the explanation of the name (sharḥ al-ism), like asking 'What is khala'?' (a mythical creature) without believing it exists. Once existence is known, the inquirer wants a real definition (conception of the essence). The author then illustrates the process with the example of a palm tree. The answer begins with the proximate genus ('tree'), but the questioner is not satisfied and asks 'Which tree?' to get the differentia (the fruit-bearing quality). Once the differentia is given, the questioning ends unless a term in the answer is not understood. In that case, one asks about that term, and the process is repeated with its genus and differentia, moving to higher genera (plant, growing body, body extending in three dimensions) until the questioning naturally stops when the meanings are clear. This demonstrates that definition is a stepwise process from the most specific to the most general, ensuring complete understanding.

Key Terms

الجنس القريب
فصل

C) 'Why is it a tree?'
D) 'What is its genus?'

Key Takeaway
A proper definition proceeds from the nearest genus to the specific differentia, and any unclear term is itself defined by the same method until all terms are understood.

Infinite Regress in Definition Resolved through Self-Evident Concepts
Author resolves the infinite regress objection to definition by analogy with syllogistic premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how definition terminates in self-evident concepts using the analogy with syllogistic premises, and apply this resolution to a real definition in an Islamic science.

Core Idea
Definition does not regress infinitely; it ends at self-evident concepts, just as proof ends in first principles.

A common objection against definition is that it leads to an infinite regress: each term in a definition must itself be defined, and so on endlessly, which is impossible. Alternatively, if one stops arbitrarily, that seems unjustified. The author of this passage offers a decisive response: the regress is not infinite because it terminates in concepts that are known by themselves (self-evident). He strengthens his answer by drawing an analogy with demonstrative proof, which also rests on first principles. This lesson will guide you through his argument and show you how it safeguards the possibility of definition in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (tasalsul)
The objector presents a dilemma: if every part of a definition requires another definition, you get an infinite regress (impossible), but if you stop somewhere arbitrarily, that is unjustified. Locate this dilemma in the Arabic text.
2
Extract the author's resolution
The author denies infinite regress. He states that the chain of definitions ends at genera and differentia that are 'necessarily known' to the questioner. These are self-evident concepts (awwaliyyat) that require no further definition. Find the phrase 'أجناس وفصول تكون معلومة للسائل لا محالة'.
The resolution does not require arbitrary stopping; it stops at what is inherently clear.
3
Understand the analogy with syllogistic premises
The author compares the end of definition to the premises of a syllogism. Just as a proof ultimately rests on first principles (e.g., 'the whole is greater than the part'), so definition rests on self-evident genera and differentia. Identify the sentence 'فآخر الحدّ يجري مجرى مقدّمات القياس' and reflect on how the analogy works.
The analogy shows that both definition and proof have a foundational layer that is accepted without further justification.
4
Apply the resolution to an Islamic science example
Consider the definition of 'hukm shar'i' (legal ruling) in Usul al-Fiqh: 'Khitab al-Shar' al-muta'alliq bi-af'al al-mukallafin' (the address of the Lawgiver concerning the actions of the morally responsible). The genus ('khitab') and differentia ('muta'alliq bi-af'al al-mukallafin') are understood through self-evident concepts like 'address' and 'action'. Trace how this definition avoids regress.
If challenged to define 'khitab', one would eventually reach terms so basic that they are grasped without definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فمتى ينقطع؟ فإن تسلسل إلى غير نهاية فهو محال، وإن تعيّن توقفه فهو تحكّم . فنقول : لا يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية بل ينتهي إلى أجناس وفصول تكون معلومة للسائل لا محالة، فإن تجاهل أبداً لم يمكن تعريفه بالحد لأن كلّ تعريف وتعرّف فيستدعي معرفة سابقة، فلم يعرف صورة الشيء بالحدّ إلا أجزاء الحدّ من الجنس والفصل قبله، إما بنفسه لوضوحه وإما بتحديد آخر إلى أن يرتقي إلى أوائل عرفت بنفسها، كما أن كلّ تعلم تصديقي بالحجة فبعلم قد سبق أولية لم تعرف بالقياس أو عرفت بالقياس، ولكن تنتهي بالآخرة إلى الأوليات، فآخر الحدّ يجري مجرى مقدّمات القياس من غير فرق .
If it is said: 'When does the questioning stop? For if it goes on ad infinitum it is impossible, and if a stop is determined it is arbitrary.' We say: It does not go on ad infinitum, but ends in genera and differentia that are necessarily known to the questioner. For if he were always ignorant, it would not be possible to define him by a definition, because every definition and being defined requires prior knowledge. Thus one knows the form of a thing by definition only if the parts of the definition—the genus and differentia—are known before it, either by themselves due to their clarity or by another definition, until one ascends to first things known by themselves. Just as every assenting learning by proof requires prior knowledge, whether it is first principles not known by syllogism or known by syllogism, but ultimately ends in first principles. So the end of definition runs in the vein of premises of syllogism without difference.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a classic objection-and-answer regarding the possibility of definition. The objector raises a dilemma: if every part of a definition must itself be defined, there will be an infinite regress (impossible), or one must arbitrarily stop (unjustified). The author responds by denying the infinite regress. The regress actually terminates in concepts that are self-evident (known by themselves) to the questioner. The process of definition presupposes prior knowledge of the genus and differentia used; that prior knowledge may be immediate (self-evident) or itself obtained through another definition, but ultimately one reaches first principles (awāʾil) that are known without definition. This is analogous to demonstrative knowledge (taṣdīqī): the chain of proofs ends in first premises known by themselves (e.g., axioms). The last step in definition corresponds to the first premises of a syllogism. Thus definition is not an endless process; it rests on foundational, self-evident concepts.

Definition Requires Genus and Differentia
Author asserts that real definition consists of genus and essential differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a real definition (ḥadd) from a nominal explanation or synonym by checking for genus and essential differentia.

Core Idea
A real definition is not a synonym – it must reveal essence through genus and differentia.

In Islamic logic, a real definition (ḥadd) is not just any explanation of a word; it must capture the essence of a thing by stating its genus and essential differentia. The author here clarifies that mere synonyms or accidental descriptions are insufficient. This distinction is crucial for precise reasoning in the Islamic sciences, where definitions must be exact to avoid confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What is the term being defined? The author mentions examples like 'wine' (khamr), 'knowledge' (ʿilm), 'motion' (ḥaraka), and 'the existent' (al-mawjūd). Pick one and note what the definiendum is.
The definiendum is the thing whose essence we seek.
2
Check for a genus
Does the definiendum fall under a broader category? For instance, 'wine' falls under 'drink' (sharāb) or 'intoxicant' (al-ʿaqār). But the author says that if you merely replace it with a synonym like 'intoxicant', you are not providing a genus – you are just renaming. A true genus is a broader essential category.
If the term is the highest genus (like 'the existent'), it has no genus and thus cannot be defined.
3
Check for an essential differentia
What makes the definiendum distinct from other members of its genus? The author distinguishes differentia that are essential (constituting the quiddity) from accidental or concomitant distinctions. For a real definition, the differentia must be essential. For example, 'garment-ness' is not constituted by color or length.
Essential differentia is necessary for a real definition; accidental features are not sufficient.
4
Distinguish from synonym or description
If you only supply a synonym (e.g., 'wine is intoxicant') or a description (e.g., 'the existent is what is found'), you are not giving a real definition. The author calls this 'explanation of the name' (sharḥ al-ism) and states that calling it a definition is a different usage. Test your definition: does it include genus and essential differentia? If not, it is not a ḥadd.
Synonyms and descriptions do not reveal the essence; they are merely alternative names or accidental properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود من هذا أن الحد يتركب لا محالة من جنس الشيء وفصله الذاتي ولا معنى له سواه، وما ليس له فصل وجنس فليس له حد، ولذلك إذا سُئلنا حد الموجود لم نقدر عليه، إلا أن يراد شرح الاسم فيترجم بعبارة أخرى عجمية أو تبدل في العربية بشيء، ولا يكون ذلك حداً بل هو ذكر اسم بدل اسم آخر مرادف له، فإذا سُئلنا عن حدّ الخمر فقلنا : العقار وعن حدّ العلم فقلنا : هو المعرفة وعن حدّ الحركة فقلنا : هو النقلة، لم يكن حدا بل كان تكراراً للأشياء المترادفة، أحب أن يُسمّيه حدا فلا حرج في الإطلاقات ونحن نعني بالحد ما يحصل في النفس صورة موازية للمحدود مطابقة لجميع فصوله الذاتية. وإنما راعينا الفصول الذاتية لأن الشيء قد ينفصل عن غيره بالعَرَض الذي لا يقوم ذاته انفصال الثوب الأحمر عن الأسود، وقد ينفصل بلازم لا يفارق انفصال القار بالسواد عن الثلج وانفصال الغراب عن الببغاء، وقد ينفصل بالذات انفصال الثوب عن السيف وانفصال ثوب من إبريسم عن درهم من قطن، ومن يسأل عن ماهية الثوب طالباً حده فإنما يطلب الأمور التي بها قوام ثوبيته لأنا لا نقوم الثوبية من اللون والطول والعرض فجوابه بما لا يقوم ذات الثوب مُخِلٌّ بالسؤال، فقد عرفت أن الحد مركب من الجنس والفصل، وأن ما لا يدخل تحت جنس حتى ينفصل عنه بفصل ما لا حد له مثل ما يذكر في معرض رسم أو شرح اسم، فتسميته حدا مخالف للتسمية اصطلحنا عليها فيكون الحدّ مشتركاً له ولما ذكرناه .
And the intended meaning of this is that the definition is necessarily composed of the genus of the thing and its essential differentia, and there is no meaning for it other than that. And what does not have a differentia and genus has no definition. Therefore, if we are asked for the definition of 'the existent', we are not able to do it, unless what is intended is the explanation of the name, in which case it is translated into another non-Arabic phrase or substituted with something in Arabic, and that is not a definition; rather it is mentioning a name instead of another synonym for it. So if we are asked for the definition of wine (khamr) and we say 'al-ʿaqār' (intoxicant), and for the definition of knowledge (ʿilm) and we say 'al-maʿrifa', and for the definition of motion (ḥaraka) and we say 'al-naqla', it is not a definition but a repetition of synonyms. If someone likes to call it a definition, there is no harm in usages, but we mean by 'definition' what produces in the soul a form parallel to the defined thing, matching all its essential differentiae. And we only consider the essential differentiae because a thing may be distinguished from others by an accident that does not constitute its essence, like the distinction of a red garment from a black one; and it may be distinguished by an inseparable necessary concomitant, like distinction of pitch by blackness from snow, and distinction of a crow from a parrot; and it may be distinguished by essence, like the distinction of a garment from a sword, and a silk garment from a cotton dirham. And whoever asks about the quiddity of a garment seeking its definition, he only asks for the matters by which its garment-ness subsists, because we do not make garment-ness subsist from colour, length, and breadth. So answering him by what does not constitute the essence of the garment violates the question. So you know that definition is composed of genus and differentia, and that what does not fall under a genus so as to be distinguished from it by a differentia has no definition, like what is mentioned in the context of a description or an explanation of a name. To call it a definition is contrary to the terminology we have adopted; thus 'definition' is equivocal between it and what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on definition by reiterating its core composition: every real definition must consist of genus (jins) and essential differentia (faṣl dhātī). Things that lack a genus and differentia—such as 'the existent' (al-mawjūd), which is the most general concept—cannot be defined in the strict sense. If one attempts to define such terms, it is merely a nominal explanation (sharḥ al-ism), substituting a synonym. The author gives examples: defining wine as al-ʿaqār (intoxicant), knowledge as maʿrifa, motion as naqla—these are synonyms, not real definitions. Real definition produces a mental image matching the thing's essential differentiae. Then the author distinguishes three types of distinction: (1) by accident (e.g., red vs. black garment), (2) by inseparable necessary concomitant (e.g., pitch's blackness vs. snow's whiteness), (3) by essence (e.g., garment vs. sword). Only the last concerns the quiddity (māhiyya). For example, asking for the definition of a garment seeks what constitutes its 'garmentness', not accidental features like color. Finally, the author emphasizes that things not falling under a genus have no definition; calling a description or nominal explanation a definition is a different usage—equivocal. Thus the term 'definition' (ḥadd) has multiple meanings, but the technical one used here requires genus and differentia.

Types of Definition (Hadd) According to Demonstration
Author enumerates five types of definition (only four detailed) by their relation to demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four enumerated types of definition based on their relation to demonstration and essence.

Core Idea
Definition has multiple roles: explain name, serve as principle or result of proof, or combine both.

In this passage, the author introduces a classification of definitions based on their epistemic function. Not all definitions are alike: some merely explain what a word means, while others capture the essence or serve as building blocks of demonstrations. Understanding this classification is key to using definitions correctly in Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first type: nominal definition
This type explains the meaning of a name without regard to the existence of the thing. If existence is later confirmed, it becomes a definition of the essence.
The author emphasizes that the definition of the name may later be recognized as a definition of the essence.
2
Identify the second type: definition as result of demonstration
This is a definition that forms the conclusion of a proof, such as defining an effect through its cause.
It is called natijat burhan (result of demonstration).
3
Identify the third type: definition as principle of demonstration
This definition serves as a premise or middle term in a proof, providing the basis for demonstration.
It is called mabda' burhan (principle of demonstration).
4
Identify the fourth type: complete definition combining principle and result
This definition includes both the cause (principle) and the effect (result) of a demonstration, as in the eclipse example: effacement of light (result) and earth interposition (principle).
The author notes that this type encompasses both roles, making it a complete definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع: في أقسام الحد · والحد يطلق بالتشكيك على خمسة : الأول : الحدّ الشارح لمعنى الاسم، ولا يلتفت فيه إلى وجود الشيء وعدمه، بل ربما يكون مشكوكاً. ونذكر الحدّ ثم إن ظهر وجوده عرف أن الحد لم يكن بحسب الاسم المجرد وشرحه بل هو عنوان الذات وشرحه . الثاني : بحسب الذات وهو نتيجة برهان . الثالث : ما هو بحسب الذات وهو مبدأ برهان . الرابع : ما هو بحسب الذات. والحدّ التام الجامع لما هو مبدأ برهان ونتيجة برهان، كما إذا سُئلت عن حدّ الكُسوف فقلت : امحاء ضوء القمر لتوسط الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، فامحاءُ ضوء القمر هو نتيجة برهان وتوسط الأرض المبدأ، فإنك في معرض البرهان تقول : متى توسطت الأرض فانمحى النور فيكون التوسط حدا أوسط فهو مبدأ برهان وإلَّا انمحى حد أكبر فهو نتيجة برهان. ولذلك.
Chapter Four: On the Divisions of Definition. Definition is predicated by equivocation (tashkīk) in five ways: First: the definition that explains the meaning of the name, without regard to the existence or non-existence of the thing; indeed it may be doubtful. We mention the definition, then if its existence becomes apparent, we know that the definition was not according to the mere name and its explanation, but rather it is a designation of the essence and its explanation. Second: According to the essence, and it is the result of a demonstration. Third: That which is according to the essence, and it is the principle of a demonstration. Fourth: That which is according to the essence, and it is the complete definition encompassing both the principle of demonstration and the result of demonstration. For example, if you are asked about the definition of an eclipse and you say: 'The effacement of the moon's light due to the earth's interposition between it and the sun.' So the effacement of the moon's light is the result of a demonstration, and the interposition of the earth is the principle. For in the context of demonstration you say: 'Whenever the earth interposes, the light is effaced.' So the interposition is the middle term, being the principle of demonstration, and the effacement is the major term, being the result of demonstration. So for that...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the types of definition. The author states that the term 'definition' (ḥadd) is used in five ways by equivocation (tashkīk, meaning analogous but not univocal). However, the text as provided only details four types, likely due to OCR truncation. The first type is the nominal definition (sharḥ al-ism) which explains the meaning of the name irrespective of existence; if later existence is established, it becomes a real definition of the essence. The second type is a definition that is the result of a demonstration (natījat burhān), meaning it is the conclusion of a proof (like a definition of an effect through its cause). The third is the principle of a demonstration (mabdaʾ burhān), i.e., the definition that serves as the premise. The fourth is the complete definition that combines both the principle and the result, as in the example of an eclipse (kusūf): the definition 'effacement of moonlight due to earth's interposition' includes both the effect (effacement) and the cause (interposition). In demonstration, the middle term (ḥadd awsaṭ) is the cause, and the major term (ḥadd akbar) is the effect. The fifth type, though not enumerated here, would typically be a definition that is neither principle nor result, like a description. This classification shows that definitions can serve different roles in scientific knowledge, mirroring the roles of terms in a syllogism.

مبدأ برهان

Key Takeaway
Definitions can be classified by their role in demonstration: nominal, as result, as principle, or combined, which is crucial for scientific and theological reasoning.

Definition of a True Definition (Hadd) and Its Essential Components
Author defines true definition as mental image matching all essential differentiae, rejecting synonyms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a genuine definition (hadd) from a synonym or a description, and identify the essential differentiae that constitute a definition.

Core Idea
A definition must capture the essence, not just swap words.

The author begins by warning against a common pitfall: mistaking a synonym for a definition. He insists that a true definition is a mental image that includes all essential differentiae. In this lesson, you will learn how to identify a real definition and why essential differentiae are crucial.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the definiendum and the proposed definition
In the passage, the author gives examples: wine defined as 'al-'aqar', knowledge as 'cognition', motion as 'translocation'. Identify what is being defined and what is given as the definition.
2
Test if the proposed definition is a synonym
Check if the definiens is simply a different word for the same concept without adding new information. The author says this is not a definition but a repetition of synonymous things.
3
Identify the essential differentiae required
The author states that a definition must match all essential differentiae (fusuul dhaatiyya). These are the qualities that constitute the essence and distinguish the thing from others.
4
Distinguish essential from accidental and necessary properties
The author gives examples: a red garment vs black (accidental color), blackness of pitch vs snow (necessary but not essential), garment vs sword (essential). Only essential differentiae count in a definition.
5
Conclude whether the proposed definition is genuine
Apply the criteria: if the definition only provides a synonym or an accidental property, it is not a true hadd. A true hadd must include the genus and essential differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبدل في العربية بشيء، ولا يكون ذلك حداً بل هو ذكر اسم بدل اسم آخر مرادف له، فإذا سُئلنا عن حدّ الخمر فقلنا : العقار وعن حدّ العلم فقلنا : هو المعرفة وعن حدّ الحركة فقلنا : هو النقلة، لم يكن حدا بل كان تكراراً للأشياء المترادفة، أحب أن يُسمّيه حدا فلا حرج في الإطلاقات ونحن نعني بالحد ما يحصل في النفس صورة موازية للمحدود مطابقة لجميع فصوله الذاتية. وإنما راعينا الفصول الذاتية لأن الشيء قد ينفصل عن غيره بالعَرَض الذي لا يقوم ذاته انفصال الثوب الأحمر عن الأسود، وقد ينفصل بلازم لا يفارق انفصال القار بالسواد عن الثلج وانفصال الغراب عن الببغاء، وقد ينفصل بالذات انفصال الثوب عن السيف وانفصال ثوب من إبريسم عن درهم من قطن، ومن يسأل عن ماهية الثوب طالباً حده فإنما يطلب الأمور التي بها قوام ثوبيته لأنا لا نقوم الثوبية من اللون والطول والعرض فجوابه بما لا يقوم ذات الثوب مُخِلٌّ بالسؤال، فقد عرفت أن الحد مركب من الجنس والفصل، وأن ما لا يدخل تحت جنس حتى ينفصل عنه بفصل ما لا حد له مثل ما يذكر في معرض رسم أو شرح اسم، فتسميته حدا مخالف للتسمية اصطلحنا عليها فيكون الحدّ مشتركاً له ولما ذكرناه.
It changes in Arabic by something, and that is not a definition but rather it is mentioning a name in place of another synonymous name. So if we are asked about the definition of wine and we say 'al-'aqar', and about the definition of knowledge and we say 'it is cognition', and about the definition of motion and we say 'it is translocation', it is not a definition but rather a repetition of synonymous things. One may like to call it a definition, no harm in such expressions. But we mean by definition what arises in the soul as an image parallel to the defined, matching all its essential differentiae. We only took into account the essential differentiae because a thing may be separated from another by an accident that does not constitute its essence, like the separation of a red garment from a black one; and it may be separated by an inseparable necessary property, like the separation of pitch by blackness from snow and the separation of a crow from a parrot; and it may be separated essentially, like the separation of a garment from a sword and the separation of a silk garment from a cotton dirham. Whoever asks about the quiddity of a garment seeking its definition only seeks the matters by which its garment-ness subsists, because we do not constitute garment-ness from color, length, and width. So answering him with what does not constitute the essence of the garment is deficient with respect to the question. You have known that the definition is composed of genus and differentia, and that what does not fall under a genus so as to be separated from it by a differentia has no definition, like what is mentioned in the context of a description (rasm) or explanation of a name. So calling it a definition contradicts the terminology we have agreed upon. Thus the term 'definition' is shared (mushtarak) between this and what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the first art on the rules of definitions. The author begins by critiquing a common error: substituting a synonym for a definition. For example, if asked to define 'wine', saying 'al-'aqar' (another word for wine) does not constitute a definition because it merely repeats the meaning without analyzing the essence. Similarly for knowledge and motion. The author then states his own technical meaning: a definition is a mental image that corresponds exactly to the defined thing, including all its essential differentiae. He emphasizes essential differentiae because a thing can be distinguished from others by accidental features (like color of a garment) or by necessary properties (like blackness of pitch) but these do not define the essence. Only the essential differentia that constitutes the quiddity counts. For example, if asked to define 'garment', the answer must include what makes it a garment, not its color or dimensions. The author concludes that a true definition is composed of genus and differentia, and that things without a genus (like simple concepts) cannot have a true definition; they can only be described or explained. Calling such a description a definition would be a different usage of the term, making it ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
A true definition (hadd) must capture the essence of a thing through its essential differentiae, not through synonyms or accidental descriptions.

Hadd and Rasm

The Five Types of Definition (Hadd) in Islamic Logic
Author enumerates five types of definition using tashkik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given definition from Islamic sciences into one of the five types of hadd based on its content and function.

Core Idea
Hadd is not one thing: it ranges from a mere name explanation to a causal definition combining premise and conclusion.

In this passage, the author explains that the term 'hadd' (definition) is used in five distinct senses, arranged by tashkik (graded predication). Understanding these types is crucial for analyzing definitions used in Islamic disciplines such as kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh. This lesson will walk you through each type, from the mere explanation of a name to the definition that combines cause and effect.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the concept of tashkik
Recognize that 'hadd' is not univocal; it applies to five types with priority and posteriority, ranging from nominal to causal.
Tashkik is systematic ambiguity, not confusion.
2
Identify the first type (nominal)
This is the explanatory definition (hadd sharih) that clarifies the meaning of a name without assuming existence of the thing.
It is like a dictionary definition.
3
Distinguish types two and three
Type two is the result of a demonstration (natijat burhan), e.g., 'eclipse is obliteration of light'. Type three is the principle (mabda' burhan), e.g., 'eclipse is interposition of earth'.
One is effect, the other cause.
4
Recognize the fourth type (complete)
This combines both cause and effect, e.g., 'eclipse is obliteration due to interposition'.
Also known as hadd tamm.
5
Spot the fifth type
For simple entities like point and unity that have no causes; their definition is not derived from demonstration.
Not merely nominal, but a real definition of a simple essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحد يطلق بالتشكيك على خمسة : الأول : الحدّ الشارح لمعنى الاسم، ولا يلتفت فيه إلى وجود الشيء وعدمه، بل ربما يكون مشكوكاً. ونذكر الحدّ ثم إن ظهر وجوده عرف أن الحد لم يكن بحسب الاسم المجرد وشرحه بل هو عنوان الذات وشرحه . الثاني : بحسب الذات وهو نتيجة برهان . والثالث : ما هو بحسب الذات وهو مبدأ برهان . والرابع : ما هو بحسب الذات. والحدّ التام الجامع لما هو مبدأ برهان ونتيجة برهان، كما إذا سُئلت عن حدّ الكُسوف فقلت : امحاء ضوء القمر لتوسط الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، فامحاءُ ضوء القمر هو نتيجة برهان وتوسط الأرض المبدأ، فإنك في معرض البرهان تقول : متى توسطت الأرض فانمحى النور فيكون التوسط حدا أوسط فهو مبدأ برهان وإلَّا انمحى حد أكبر فهو نتيجة برهان. ولذلك يتداخل البرهان والحد فإن العلل الذاتية من هذا الجنس تدخل في حدود الأشياء كما تدخل في براهينها فكل ما له علة فلا بد من ذكر علته الذاتية في حده لتتم صورة ذاته، وقد تدخل العلل الأربعة في حدّ الشيء الذي له العلل الأربعة كقوله في حد القادوم : إنه آلة صناعية من حديد، شكله كذا يقطع به الخشب نحتاً؛ فقولك : آلة جنس وصناعية تدلّ على المبدأ الفاعل، والشكل يدلّ على الصورة، والحديد يدلّ على المادة والنحت على الغاية، وبه الاحتراز عن المثقب والمنشار إذ لا ينحت بهما. وقد يقتصر في الحدّ على نتيجة البرهان إذا حصل التمييز بها فيقال: حدّ الكسوف انمحاء ضوء القمر، فيسمى هذا حدا نتيجة برهان وإن اقتصر على العلّة وقال : الكسوف هو توسط الأرض بين القمر وبين الشمس، وحصل به التمييز قيل حدّ مبدأ برهان والحد التام المركب منهما . الخامس : ما هو حدّ لأمور ليس لها علل وأسباب ولو كان لها علل لكانت عللها غير داخلة في جواهرها كتحديد النقطة والوحدة والحدّ ، فإن الوحدة يذكر لها تعريف وليس للوحدة سبب، والحدّ يحدّ فإنه قول دالّ على ماهية الشيء، وللقول فإنه حادث لا محالة لعلّة لكن مُسبِّبه ليس ذاتياً له كانمحاء ضوء القمر في الكسوف، فهذا الخامس ليس بمجرّد شرح الاسم فقط، ولا هو مبدأ برهان ولا نتيجة برهان ولا هو مركب منهما ؛ فهذه أقسام ما يطلق عليه اسم الحد، وقد يُسمّى الرسم حدا على أنه مميز فيكون ذلك وجهاً سادساً .
And 'definition' is predicated by tashkik upon five: First: the definition that explains the meaning of the name, without regard to the existence or non-existence of the thing; indeed it may be doubtful. We mention the definition, then if its existence appears, it is known that the definition was not according to the mere name and its explanation, but rather it is the designation of the essence and its explanation. Second: according to the essence, and it is the result of a demonstration. Third: what is according to the essence, and it is the principle of a demonstration. Fourth: what is according to the essence, and it is the complete definition combining what is the principle of a demonstration and the result of a demonstration, as when you are asked about the definition of eclipse and you say: 'the obliteration of the moon's light due to the interposition of the earth between it and the sun'. So the obliteration of the moon's light is the result of a demonstration, and the interposition of the earth is the principle. For in the context of demonstration you say: whenever the earth interposes, then the light is obliterated. So the interposition becomes the middle term, which is the principle of the demonstration, and the obliteration becomes the major term, which is the result. Therefore demonstration and definition overlap; for essential causes of this kind enter into the definitions of things as they enter into their demonstrations. So everything that has a cause must have its essential cause mentioned in its definition to complete the image of its essence. And the four causes may enter into the definition of a thing that has four causes, as in the definition of an adze: it is an artificial tool made of iron, shaped in such a way, used to cut wood by carving. So your saying 'tool' is the genus, 'artificial' indicates the efficient cause, 'shape' indicates the formal cause, 'iron' indicates the material cause, and 'carving' indicates the final cause; and by this it is distinguished from the awl and the saw, for they are not used for carving. And sometimes one may confine the definition to the result of the demonstration if distinction is obtained by it: it is said 'the definition of eclipse is the obliteration of the moon's light', and this is called a definition as the result of demonstration. If one confines it to the cause and says 'the eclipse is the interposition of the earth between the moon and the sun', and distinction is obtained, it is called a definition as the principle of demonstration. And the complete definition is composed of both. Fifth: what is a definition for things that have no causes and reasons; and if they had causes, their causes would not be included in their essences, like the definition of point, unity, and definition itself. For unity has a definition given for it, but unity has no cause. And definition is defined: it is a statement indicating the quiddity of a thing. But the statement is necessarily originated, and has a cause, but its efficient cause is not essential to it, like the obliteration of the moon's light in the eclipse. So this fifth is not merely an explanation of the name, nor is it a principle of demonstration nor a result of demonstration nor composed of both. These are the divisions of what is called definition. And 'description' (rasm) may be called a definition, as it is a distinguisher, so that would be a sixth aspect.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This chapter enumerates the five types of definition (hadd) explained by tashkik (graded predication). The first type is a nominal definition that clarifies the meaning of a term without assuming the thing's existence; it is essentially a lexical explanation. The second type is a definition that is the conclusion of a demonstration (natijat burhan), i.e., the definiendum's essence is known via a proof. The third type is the definition that serves as the premise (mabda' burhan) of a demonstration, i.e., the essential cause. The fourth type combines both: it includes both the cause and the effect, as in the definition of a lunar eclipse stating both the interposition of the earth (cause) and the obliteration of light (effect). The author explains that demonstration and definition merge here because essential causes are part of both. He further illustrates how the four causes (material, formal, efficient, final) can all enter a definition, using the example of an adze (qadum). The definition mentions genus (tool), matter (iron), form (shape), agent (artificial), and purpose (carving). This distinguishes the adze from other tools. The fifth type is for simple entities that have no causes (like point, unity, and definition itself). Such definitions are not merely nominal nor derived from demonstration. The chapter ends by noting that 'description' (rasm) is sometimes called a definition, making a sixth kind.

الحد الشارح
نتيجة برهان
مبدأ برهان
العلل الأربعة
الرسم

Key Takeaway
The five types of hadd are predicated by tashkik: nominal, result of demonstration, principle of demonstration, composite, and essential definition of simple entities.

Hadd and Rasm

The Complete Definition and the Four Causes in Hadd
Author illustrates the complete definition using the example of an adze and the four causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes (material, formal, efficient, final) in a given definition from Islamic sciences and construct a complete definition using them.

Core Idea
A complete definition includes all essential causes — just as the adze is defined by its matter, form, agent, and purpose.

The author explains that for things that have four causes, a complete definition includes all of them, as demonstrated by the definition of an adze (qadum). This lesson teaches you to recognize and apply the four-cause analysis to definitions in Islamic sciences, particularly in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four causes
Material (what it is made of), formal (its shape/structure), efficient (who or what makes it), final (its purpose).
These are from Aristotelian physics, used widely in Islamic philosophy.
2
Analyze the adze example
The author defines an adze as: 'artificial tool made of iron, shaped in such a way, used to cut wood by carving.' Identify each cause: tool is genus, 'artificial' is efficient, 'shape' is formal, 'iron' is material, 'carving' is final.
The genus is not a cause but the broader category.
3
Understand why complete definition needs both cause and effect
The eclipse example shows that a complete definition includes both the cause (interposition) and the effect (obliteration). This is the fourth type of hadd.
Cause and effect are essential for demonstration.
4
Practice on a new example
Take something like 'sunnah' (a practice) and try to define it with its four causes: what is its material (following the Prophet), form (specific actions), efficient (the Prophet's example), final (gaining reward).
Not all things have clear four causes; use judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And 'definition' is predicated by tashkik upon five: First: the definition that explains the meaning of the name, without regard to the existence or non-existence of the thing; indeed it may be doubtful. So the interposition becomes the middle term, which is the principle of the demonstration, and the obliteration becomes the major term, which is the result. Commentary
This chapter enumerates the five types of definition (hadd) explained by tashkik (graded predication). The first type is a nominal definition that clarifies the meaning of a term without assuming the thing's existence; it is essentially a lexical explanation. The second type is a definition that is the conclusion of a demonstration (natijat burhan), i.e., the definiendum's essence is known via a proof. The third type is the definition that serves as the premise (mabda' burhan) of a demonstration, i.e., the essential cause.
